FOR THE RED RAGE.
The Procession of Egos.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
On each side of him for many a mile extended a
pebbled beach shut in by hazy headlands. The
quiet sea chafed gently at his feet on a myriad
little stones—once hugest boulders, now worn by
unwearied Time into minutest and most diverse
shapes.
lie stooped and separated one stone from its
fellows—it was so very circular. It was somewhat
flat ; about the size of a small coin.
Coincidence has done this, he thought ; or
rather Chance. My finding it makes the coinci-
dence. Yes : Chance alone has formed it so.
Then . . . what chance ?
The Philosopher put the little stone disc in his
pocket and walked home, considering deeply. He
proceeded at once to his laboratory and measured
the granite fragment with his most delicate in-
struments. To their mechanism, capable of dis-
criminating to a thousandth part of an inch, the
circle of the thing was round, try it which way he
would —round absolutely. The Philosopher was
disturbed.
He leaned his head upon his hands, with his
eyes fixed upon the insignificant pebble placed on
the table before him.
“It seems to me,” he mused, “it is no mere
speculation. It is a certainty—it must be. Yet I
will place the argument on paper so that no error
may creep in.”
He was trembling as he took a pen and wrote.
* * * *
Either Something exists, or Nothing. If No-
thing exists, conceptions cannot exist. But I can
conceive existence. Therefore my conception
exists.
Yet my conception cannot exist apart from Me.
Therefore I exist.
And I am Something—Matter, Spirit, what you
please, Therefore Something exists, I will call
that Something Matter.
Existence implies location in Space. If a thing
existed nowhere it would not exist. Therefore
it must exist somewhere.
Existence implies location in Time. If a thing
existed for no-time it would not exist. There-
fore it must exist some-time.
But the conception of Space implies an infinity
of Space. If Space be not infinite, it must have a
material boundary. But if that boundary be not
infinite, itself must have a material boundary,
and so on —to infinity.
And, for similar reasons, the conception of Time
implies an infinity of Time.
Therefore Matter exists in an infinity of Space
and Time.
But if Space and Time be infinite, why not
Matter? The eternal repetition of Matter is at
least as likely as its sudden cessation. So far as
human knowledge extends, Matter is eternally
repeated.
And, so far as human knowledge extends, Matter
is identical in essence. There is at least as much
likelihood that its primal elements will remain the
same as there is that they will change to some-
thing inconceivably different.
I may assume, therefore, that there exists an
Infinity of Identical Matter in an Infinity of Space
and Time.
That Matter must be held or governed by a
condition of its own existence—since Space and
Time are abstractions. I will call that condition
Law.
And as Matter is identical, the Law which
governs it must also be identical.
This pebble in all its remarkable rotundity was
formed by what we call Chance, which in its last
analysis is only an expression of Law, and is cer-
tain and entirely inflexible.
Given Sea, and Stone, and Time long enough, a
shape such as this must have been formed.
And if this shape, in the changes which Time
never-ending must bring, then every and any
other shape.
But Time is not operating in its chances on this
World alone, but on all other Worlds which it has
formed, extending in Space inimitably.
Therefore other discs the very counterpart of
this must exist in other Worlds.
But, the Worlds being unlimited in number, the
perfect discs must be unlimited.
And all other shapes must be as limitless in
number as the Worlds themselves.
But Chance must repeat the shape and charac-
teristics of this World as it repeats the discs.
Some Worlds, therefore, Chance must have made
in physical formation exactly similar to this.
And, as the Worlds are unlimited, and Time has
never begun nor yet can end, some World must
be so like that the similarity will extend to every
islet, to every rock, to every pebble, and to that
pebble’s exact position on the sea-beach as,.l
found this.
Chance, then, in its infinite variety, now holds
sway over a World the precise replica of this in
everything—with like human beings, like laws,
customs, languages, and religions—like to the
tiniest grain of sand, and to the most transient
thought flickering through a brain—a perfect
identity.
There must be therefore a Man like the —of my
name—of my past—a Man who is now writing
what I am now writing—who is now thinking
what I am now thinking—who picked up lately,
as I did, a remarkable pebble.
But, | as there is a limitless series of Worlds
subject to Chance, there must be a limitless series
of Worlds such as this.
Andi there must as necessarily be an infinite
series of
• * * *
He broke off here—the room seemed so small
and its confines so insupportable. He went out
into the night wind, which enveloped him with its
grateful chill.
A swart cloud hung sullen under the face of the
moon. Before him there crouched in the dark-
ness kn undefined mass. -It was a rugged hill,
strewn with rocks and smoothed boulders. He
gaine'd the summit, and bared his head to the
stronger breeze. The cloud had moved and no
longer prevented the moon’s light.
And the Philosopher now became aware of a
rhythmic murmur— not loud and thundering, but
enormous in its pervasiveness. Tramp!— it
sounded . . . tramp ! . . . tramp!
The man looked at the landscape below and saw
at first dimly, then quite distinctly, an expanse of
people. It was the noise of their walking he had
heard as they marched past the hill with measured
and unhurried step. They entirely filled the view,
save on the side on which he stood, and they rolled
on as a sea that rolls to meet the Sea.
Looking still more intently, the Philosopher per-
ceived with a terror, the like of which he had
never imagined before, that every man of the
crowd was a likeness of himself dreadful in its
exactness.
Yet they paid no sort of Attention to him, but
continued their solemn way.
Impelled by a strange curiosity, he shouted
to them :
“ Where do I come from ? ”
He hacf intended to say—“ Where do you come
from?” but in some inexplicable way, and in
spite of himself, he was compelled to say “I”
instead of “you.”
Then all who marched below, to the most remote
of them, turned their heads backwards and peered
with contracted brows, and peered most curiously,
and said ali in one great voice, enquiringly, yet
somewhat sadly withal :
“ Where do I come from ? ”
Then he asked again, being again impelled to
say “ I” instead of “you” :
“ How far do I extend ? ”
And all the men who marched below suddenly
raised themselves a-tip-toe to look over one an-
other’s heads, and answered blankly :
“ How far do I extend ?”
And once more, notwithstanding his fear, he
asked a further question, not this time essaying to
speak the word “ you,” for it was impossible:
“ Where am I going ? ”
And at this question all the landscape of faces
with the moonlight on them turned towards him,
and looked at him with frightful mockery, and
answered :
“ Where am I going ? ”
But the sound of their voice was so loud and
pervading that the hill on which he stood quivered
to its rooted depths. Its cliffs rose from the earth
and flung themselves headlong down sheer steeps.
Massy stones lying squat starred and split to their
bellied bases. The rounded granite pebbles
crumbled into dust. The moon herself, serenest
of spheres, darkened her ray and trembled in her
silver chains. And the stars sparkled fitfully
and sought vainly to forsake their customed
paths. Ndr was the sound unfelt by the nether
and piled universes for ever.
He hastened down the quaking hillside and
joined the infinite throng.
J. F. MILLINGTON.
Reprints.
[One of the strongest poetic forces in America works
through the dialect-verse which has sprung up so abun-
dantly of late years. And, when the dialect isn’t over-
done, the appeal is instantaneous.- Some of James
Whitcomb Riley’s verse (“ Leonainie ’.’ and “When the
Frost is on the Punkin ”) was reprinted in The Bulletin
not long ago. He was born at Gianfield, in the State of
Indiana, in 63, and is still writing. He began manly
life as a sign-painter (Henry Lawson can flourish brushes
with him), then joined a company of strolling actors for
whom he would dish-up plays and write songs and verses,
and by degrees drifted into lecturing and journalism.
“ Benj. F. Johnson, of Boone,” was his beginning
pen - name ; but people gave him the title of
“ the Hoosier poet,” because he wrote chiefly in the
Hoosier or Indiana dialect. (Why “ Hoosier ” doesn’t
appear). There is in him a vein of true poetry, and
some of his un-dialect-verse is good, though not quite as
forcible as that which has made him popular. Sam Walter
Foss (The Bulletin recently reprinted his “ Hullo ! ” and
“Keep-a-Goin’ !”)is,in his best things, fully Riley’s equal,
but he has not writ ten so much or so variously. He springs
from New Hampshire, a r) is five years younger than
Riley. At last accounts he was editing the Yankee
Blade, a Boston newspaper in which most of his verse
has appeared. Mather Dean Kimbali (there are several
poetic Kimballs in America) is the oldest of the trio,
dating as far back as ’49. He hails from Wisconsin—
still.]
THE OLD MAN AND JIM.
Old man never had much to say—
’Coptic 1 to J im—
And Jim was the wildest boy he had—
And the Old Man jes’ wrapped up in him I
Never heerd him speak but once
Er twice in my life—and first time was
When the army broke out, and Jim he went,
The Old man backin’ him, fer three months.
And all ’at I heerd the Old man say
Was, jes’ as we turned to start away:
“Well ; good-bye, Jim :
Take keer of yourse’f 1 ”
'Peared-like, he was more satisfied
Jes’ lookin’ at Jim,
And likin’ him all to hisse’f-like, see?
’Cause he was jes’ wrapped up in him I
And over and over I mind the day
The Old man come and stood round in the way
While we was drillin’, a-watchin’ Jim—
Aud down at the deepot a-heerin’ him say t
“ Well; good-bye, Jim :
Take keer of yourse'f 1 ”
Never was nothin’ about the farm
Disting’ished Jim;
Neighbors all ust to wonder why
The Old man ’peared wrapped up in him.
But when Cap. Biggler, he writ back,
’At Jim was the bravest boy we had
In the whole dern rigiment, white er black,
And his fightin’ good as his farmin’ bad—
’At he had led, with a bullet clean
Bored through his thigh, and carried the flag
Through the bloodiest battle you ever seen—
The Old man wound up a letter to him
’At Cap. read to us, ’at said : “ Tell Jim
Good-bye:
And take keer of hisse’f.’
Jim come back jes’ long enough
To take the whim
’At he’d like to go back in the cavelry—
And the Old man jes’ wrapped up in him 1
Jim ’lowed ’at he’d had sich luck afore,
Guessed he’d tackle her three years more.
And the Old man give him a colt he’d raised
And follered him over to Camp Ben Wade,
And laid around fer a week er so,
Watchin’ Jim on dress-parade—
Tel finally he rid away,
And last he heerd was the Old man say—
“ Well; good-bye, Jim :
Take keer of yourse’f ! ”
Tuk the papers, the Old man did,
A-watchin : fer Jim—
Fully believin’ he’d make his mark
Some way—jes’ wrapped up in him !
And many a time the word ’u’d come
'At stirred him up like the tap of a drum—•
At Petersburg fer instance, where
Jim rid right into their cannons there,
And tuk ’em, and p’inted ’em t’other way,
And socked it home to the boys in grey,
As they skooted fer timber, and on and on—
Jim a lieutenant and one arm gone,
And the Old man’s words in his mind all day—
“ Well; good-bye, Jim :
Take keer of yourse’f ! ”
Think of a private, now, perhaps,
We’ll say like Jim,
’At’s dumb clean up to the shoulder-straps—
And the Old man jes wrapped up in him I
Think of him —with the ;war plum’ through,
And the glorious old Red-White-and-Blue
A-laughin’ the news down over Jim,
And the Old man, bendin’ over him—
The surgeon turnin’ away with tears
’At had n’t leaked fer years and years—
As the hand of the dyin’ boy clung to
His father’s, the old voice in his ears—
“ Wel l; good-bye, Jim :
Take keer of yourse’f ! ”
JAMES WHUTOOMB RILRY.
TELLIN' WHAT THE BABY DID.
In the cosy twilight hid,
Tellin’ what the baby did,
Sits Matilda every night
’Twixt the darkness and the light.
Tells me in her cutest way
All the hist’ry of the day,
Gives me all; leaves nothin’ hid,
Tellin’ me what the baby did.
Beats the whole decline and fall
Of the Roman Empire. Gol!
William Shakespeare never hed
Cuter thoughts than baby said.
An’ he hez, to sing his thoughts
Sweeter words than Isaac Watts.
Tildy, she leaves nothin’ hid
Tellin’ me what the baby did.
Pooty hard schoolmarm is Fate
To her scholars, small and great}
I hev felt upon my han’
Tingle of her sharp rattan ;
But she pities our distress,
An’ she gives a glad recess,
When Matilda sits, half-hid,
Tellin’ what the baby did.
Trudge off with my dinner pail
Every mornin’ without fail;
Work, with hardly time for breath j
Come home, tired half to death j
But I feel a perfect rest
Settle down upon niy breast,
Settin’, by the twilight hid,
Hearin’ what the baby did.
Sometimes I cannot resist,
An’ I s.,ake my doubled fisc
In the face of fate, and swear,
“ You don’t treat a fellow fair 1 ”
Then when I go home at night,
My whole system full of fight,
Tilly, she sits there, half-hid,
Tellin’ what the baby did.
Then I jest make up with fate,
An’ my happiness is great;
But if fate should lay its han*
On that baby, understan’,
Through the worl’ I’d sulk apart,
With red murder in my heart;
If she sat no more half-hid,
Tellin’ what the baby did.
S. W. FOBS.
OL’ PICKETT’S NELL.
Feel more ’an ever like a fool
Sence Pickett’s Nell come back from sohool.
She oncet wuz twelve ’nd me eighteen
(’Nd better friends you never seen)j
But now—oh, my!
She’s dressed so fine, ’nd growed so tall,
’Nd I’arnin’—she jes knows it all.
She’s eighteen now, but I’m so slow
I’m whar I wuz six year ago.
Six year ! Waal, Waal ! doan’t seem a week
Sence we rode Dolly to th’ creek,
’Nd fetched th’ cattle home at night,
Her hangin’ to my jacket tight.
But now—oh, my !
She rides in Pickett’s new coopay
Jes like she’d be’n brung up thet way,
’Nd lookin’ like a reg’lar queen—
Th’ mostest like I ever seen.
She uster tease, ’nd tease, 'nd tease
Me fer to take her on my knees;
Then tired me out ’ith Marge’y Daw,
’Nd laffin’ tell my throat wuz raw.
But now—oh, my !
She sets up this way—kinder proud,
’Nd never noways laughs out loud.
You w’u’d n’t hardly think thet 3he
Had ever see-sawed on my knee.
’Nd sometimes, ef at noon I’d choose
To find a shady place ’nd snooze,
I’d wake with burdocks in my hair
’Nd elderberries in my ear.
But now—oh, ray !
Somebody said (’t was yesterday) •
“Let’s hev some fun w’ile Ned’s away;
Let’s turn his jacket inside out; ”
But Nell—she’d jes turn red ’nd pout.
’Nd oncet when I wuz dreamin’-like,
A-throwin’ akerns in th’ dike,
She put her arms clean round my head,
’Nd whispered soft, “ I like you, Ned ; ”
But now—oh, my !
She curteseyed so stiff ’an grand,
’Nd never oncet held out her hand,
’Nd called me “ Mister Edward ! ” Laws t
Thet ain’t my name, ’nd never wuz.
’Nd them ’at lcnowed ’er years ago
Jes laughed t’ see ’er put on so ;
Ooz it wuz often talked, ’nd said,
“Nell Pickett’s jes cut out fer Ned.”
But now—oh, my !
She held her purty head so high,
’Nd skasely saw me goin’ by—
I w'’u’d n’t dast (afore last night)
A-purposely come near her sight.
Last night! Ez I wuz startin’ out
To git th’ cows, I heerd a shout;
’Nd, sure as ghostses, she wuz thar,
A-settin’ on ol’ Pickett’s mar’;
’Nd then —oh, my !
She said she’d cried fer all th’ week
To take th’ ol’ ride to th’ creek ;
Then talked about ol’ times, ’nd said,
“ Them days wuz happy, wa’n’t they, Ned ?”
Th’ folks wuz talkin’ ev’rywhars
’Bout her a puttin’ on sech airs,
’Nd seemed t’ me like tlray wuz right,
Afore th’ cows come home last night.
But now—oh, my !
MATHER DEAN KIMRALL.

T. O o KI
BEFORE YOU
T - IS -A- i
When you go out shopping, consider, think
of the money in your pocket, and take account
of the many stores whose various attractions
are laid before you. There is A, with temp-
tation in the form of a- pretty show ; there is
B, whose wiles are innumerable ; and 0,
who says that he is the man for your money ;
and so on, throughout a list as long as
George-street, each smiling dealer has some-
thing to say and to offer.
Please Pause!
Hearken not to the song of the syren, sing
she ever so sweetly; heed not the voice of
the charmer, charm he never so wisely.
Your pocket’s concerned ; and there are
pirates about. As the poet pertinently puts
it: “ There be land-sharks and water-
sharks, ” and it is for. you to see that your
dealings are with none of these voracious
monsters. Take warning in time, for it will
be no use when the new taxes come round —
and the tax-gatherer won’t take no for an
answer—for you to plead : There would have
been enough and to spare had I been wise
enough to

Various Verses .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
PYGMALION.
The afternoon is late, and holds
A sense, of golden peace ;
The roses shut their crimson folds,
Jaded with slow release.
In cold, white purity she stands,
Beyond a mortal sweet;
Down fall the tools, and with looked hands
The sculptor clasps her feet.
His heart bows in the scented gloom,
Through love and longing weak :
He knows not of the rosy bloom
On heaving breast and cheek !
Tasmania.
JAMES HEBBLETHWAITE.
THE OUTCAST.
I. /. .
Across, the night, where shadows deep .
Are slumb’ring like a wrong asleep,
I hear a constant cry of pain . . .
And, out at sea, the wind and rain.
What mean these sounds of sorrow drear ?
What mean these piteous xnoans I hear ?
What is it, Sister in the Park ?
What is it, Woman in the Dark ?
I cannot think, or work, or rest,
Because you sob and moan unblest;
I cannot live my life and know
That you are cursed in shame and woe.
II.
Cry at my heart no more ! I will
Not heed ! Depart to whence you came !
Depart ! and keep your clamour still,
For I am weary of your woes
That never cease ; your burning shame ;
Your life that dark and darker grows.
I have not made you what you are ;
I did not spurn you while you clung ;
/did not bid you stand afar.
Entreat me not; let me be free;
Of such as you the knell is rung :
Stand back ! You shall not torture me.
III.
It is no use ; I may not feign ;
I could not-shut my ears, and say„
I would not hear—all night, all day—■
Your everlasting cry of .pain.
I thought I need not heed your voice,
And built a future while I slept;
But, waking, knew that you still w;ept, ,
And had no dreams of hope or choice
Of joyous things. I flung you gold,
Yet held from you my best. But know
The truth at last: now will I go
And call you mine, your form enfold
Will kiss your lips, and soothe your c?y ;,
Your head upon my breast keep still; •
Nor cease to lift you up, until
We equal live, or fighting die.
N.S.W.
M.J.C.
EVOLUTION.
Amid the whirl of molten suns,
The tempest and the fire,
God saw his worlds by millions
Fashioned to his desire ;
And while through heaven the warm earth flew
The reign of soul began,
And gentler still and nobler grew
The brute that would be man. ,
But, ere the first clear utterance came,
With vaunting look and sign,
God saw his new-born men proclaim
Their ancestry divine;
And still the cooling earth ran round,
And, lord of beast and fruit,
Each age more fierce and cruel found > |
The man that was the brute.
’Tis true man fell, as priests avow,
But scarcely from a star — • <
Brute-born at first, and lower now
Than these our brothers are.
What tree may flaunt its boughs in air
Self-severed from the roots ? ,i
We are but men because we were
The best among the brutes..
Ah, brother brutes, forgive the sin
That thought your name disgrace;
Knowing our heirship, we, your kin,
Shall yet regain our place. ;
For slowly, slowly cools the earth
By millions yet untrod,
And still there’s time to bring to birth
The man that shall be God.
Sydney.
I.D.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
4 Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly undei stand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
ether topical drawings in line or ivash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion— i.c., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
ivhich such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, AUGUST 8, 1896
The Futility of it All.
For the last two weeks or more the Parlia-
ment and the press of New South Wales have
sawed the air furiously over Premier Reid’s
wondrous financial statement, and his alleged
surplus, and about the tangled monetary
affairs of the province generally. Except for
the riot which greeted the famous Carter
budget in Victoria a year or two ago, there
has been no such widespread curse pro-
nounced on any Treasurer’s balance-sheet
within the last decade. It has been demon-
strated, among other things, that the £330,000
surplus was created by charging about
£1,000,000 of ordinary expenditure to loans,
by taking over £300,000 out of loans and
putting it into revenue on account of certain
things which are alleged to have happened
as much as 40 years back, by charging
£216,000 of the road-and-bridge expenditure
of ’95 and ’96 to the Dibbs Government of
1893, by charging all the unpaid bills of pre-
vious years back to these years and by
charging all this year’s unpaid bills forward
to next year, by debiting the forest-thinning
job to loans and paying for the sand-shifting
business at Centennial Park out of the trust
funds, and by many other wiles and devices.
Ex-Treasurer M’Millan, who was for many
years the great brass god of Foreign-trade
finance, and whose absolute reliability on
monetary matters has been guaranteed over
and over again by the S.M. Herald and
Telegraph and Evening News, declared
in the Assembly that the surplus was a
trick and a hollow falsehood, and that
the Reid Government had had a deficit
of about £600,000 a year ever since it
came into office. And after all this ex-
posure, and the utter destruction of any
reputation as a financier which Premier Reid
might ever have possessed, and the wreckage
of his character for ingenuousness and fair-
dealing, the public may now sit down to
seriously consider the futility of it all. For
the miserable, exploded budget must needs
go through, and the “faked” estimates be
endorsed by the Assembly, because the
system of Government by Contradiction re-
quires it, and all the bustle and riot might
almost as well not have been.
* * * *
One of the principal fictions of the Parlia-
mentary system is that the Assembly has
control of the finances. As a matter of fact,
it has no control worth mentioning and no
direct control at all. If the Assembly in-
sisted upon straightening up the Reid
budget and upon putting right all the crooked
things which are in it, and on fixing the
national balance-sheet on an honest footing,
it would constitute a vote of censure and the
Reid Ministry must resign. That fact alone
would not matter much to the Freetrade
majority in the Assembly, because it could
find abundant material in the party for' a
better Ministry than the one now existing.
But the same vote which put Reid out would
put Lyne and the Protectionist party in, and
the result would be a tariff which the govern-
ing party is pledged to oppose, and the House
would have no more direct control over
Lyne’s financial doings, if he chose to cook
the national balance-sheet as Reid has done,
than it has over the ancient muddler who
now runs the Treasury. The Assembly has
to vote that Reid’s budget is quite honest
and reliable, however much it knows to the
contrary, unless it is also prepared to vote
against Foreign-trade and direct taxation,
and all the other items of the Government
policy. Not being prepared to do this, all
the criticisms levelled at the budget are
merely a grinding of the empty wind in the
mill of useless logic, and a weary slaying of
the corpse of rhetoric all over again.
* ♦ * *
The Foreign-trade party is perfectly aware
that, whatever chance its policy might have
had if run by an honest and brainy states-
man, the small police-court trickeries of
Premier Reid are dragging it down into the
mire of utter discredit. But as it can’t get
rid of its foolish incubus without abandon-
ing power and office, and handing them over
to its opponents, and pulling the whole struc-
ture down on its own head, it naturally sticks
by its leader and swears that all his doings
are perfectly right and honorable. The
Foreign-trade press does likewise for the
same reason, and laboriously whitewashes its
hated idol in massive slabs of print. The
Protectionist party press, for an exactly
similar reason, denounces everything that
Reid says or does, provided its political
figure-head, Lyne, denounces it also. When
Lyne comes into power the press which be-
longs exclusively to his party will have to
prove that everything he says or does is ab-
solutely correct, because sacrificing him
would mean sacrificing the national policy
along with him, so its position is just the
same as that of its contemporaries.
• * * *
The state of the Labor-party is, if pos-
sible, a trifle more difficult than that of the
direct Foreign-trade section of the House.
The. majority of the Labor members are
Protectionists. They are also in favor of
land and income-taxes, and when Lyne
offered them one part of the democratic pro-
gramme—Protection—and Reid offered them
the other—direct taxation—they had to
choose between the two, because under the
party system Lyne and Reid were the only
alternatives. They decided to sacrifice Pro-
tection for the sake of direct taxation, 'and
they got a land-tax which was a very weak
apology for the one they expected, and
which the present Government flatly refuses
to extend any further. Now, having sacri-
ficed Protection in order to get the land-tax,
they have to make a further sacrifice and
vote their full confidence in the honesty of
Reid’s- dishonest finance in order to keep it.
There is no practical way provided by the
constitution whereby they can vote for Pro-
tection and direct taxation, or for Protection
and direct taxation and against the glaring
manipulation of the public accounts, simply
because two extraneous party leaders, with
neither of whom the Labor members have any
connection whatever, choose to decide, for
reasons which don’t in the least interest the said
members, that these matters shall not go to-
gether. One of the standing jibes of the Free-
trade press is that the Labor-members
generally speak bitterly against the Ministry
and vote with it. In this respect they are at
least honest, which the rank-and-file of the
other parties are not. Circumstances practi-
cally compel them—or at least the party con-
siders thatcircumstancescompel them—to vote
with the Government, but circumstances don’t
necessarily compel them to lie about it by
professing to fully approve of all its doings.
The position of a party which generally
speaks against the Ministry yet votes with it
is about the most logical thing that the sys-
tem admits of when worked on a peace basis;
it simply means that the party regards the
Government as generally wrong but votes
with it because otherwise it would bring in a
Government which would, in its opinion, be
wrong still more frequently. The truly logi-
cal course would probably be for the Third
Party to put matters on a permanent war
basis by voting strictly according to its con-
science and its Democratic instincts every
time. In that case it would gain nothing in
the immediate present, but it might bring
about a political crisis once a month or so,
and the utter impossibility of the situation
might compel Parliament to abandon Party
Government altogether.
* * * *
In South Australia the Kingston Ministry
has included in its programme the creation
of an Elective Executive, and a bill to estab-
lish the same system has just got through
its second reading in Maoriland, despite
the bitter opposition of Premier Seddon.
The establishment of this system is, in
The Bulletin’s opinion, the only process by
which Parliament will ever acquire any real
power over the finances, or over anything
else. It provides that the members of the
Cabinet shall be elected for a fixed term—say
three years —unless sooner removed by a
definite vote of the House. The contract
between them and the House will contain no
tacit proviso, as at present, that Parliament
shall have blind confidence in them, and sup-
port them in all their actions until it throws
them out altogether and gives the same
blind support to another crowd equally un-
trustworthy. It will simply announce that
Parliament regards them as the best men it
can get, and guarantees their salaries, and
retains its independence. Under this system
the present situation in N.S. Wales would be
widely different from what it is. If the
Assembly considered that the Reid budget
was a gross misrepresentation, it would have
full power to alter or correct it without
thereby ejecting Reid or sacrificing Freetrade
or direct taxation, or anything else. It would
not be expressing “ want of confidence ” in
the Government, for there would be no ques-
tion of confidence, as it is now understood, in
the bargain. If the Foreign-trade majority
considered that Reid, and any other member
or members of the Ministry, were hopeless
muddlers who were bringing its policy into
disrepute, it could remove them by a vote of
the House and put better men in their
places ; removing a Ministry wouldn’t neces-
sarily mean sacrificing the whole policy of
the party and putting a totally opposite crowd
into power, and therefore no party would be
compelled to endorse the dishonest or foolish
acts of its leaders simply for its own preser-
vation.
* * * *
Under the elective system an aggressive
Premier could not bulldoze his followers into
obedience by threats of a dissolution, and
ruinous election expenses, and possible loss
of their seats ; the House could only be dis-
solved before its time by its own vote. He
couldn’t coerce them, either, by threatening to
resign and advise the Governor to send for
the possibly still more obnoxious Opposition
leader, for if he resigned the choice of his
successor would rest with the House itself.
The Labor-party wouldn’t then be compelled
to sacrifice Protection to get direct taxation
or to prop up dishonest financing to keep it.
The House could give a straight vote on
every question without thereby upsetting a
Ministry or a policy, and Reid would not be
able to ukase that the members who wanted
direct taxation must sacrifice Protection to
get it, or Lyne to decree that those who
wanted Protection could only have it by
abandoning direct taxation. No Minister
could buy office by offering concessions to
different factions, for the House itself would
be the only power which could offer any
concessions. There would not be one party
which was compelled by the exigencies of the
political system to support the Government
even when it was wrong, or another which was
called upon to denounce it even when it was
right. The House would not be virtually
compelled, as it is now, to keep the whole
Ministry or wreck the whole Ministry in
order to retain or get rid of one individual
in it about whose appointment it had not
even been consulted. The elective system
might possibly prove far from perfect, but
that, at the worst, is only a possibility. The
utter unworkableness and corruption and
chaos of the present system are absolute
certainties.
* * * *
The Labor-party is the only body in New
South Wales politics which advocates the
elective system, and even it does so in a
feeble fashion and puts this reform far down
on its programme. Yet the party has already
proved that it can do very little good under
the present order of things ; almost all it
has done, in fact, has been to secure One-
man-one-vote and a small measure of direct
taxation, and to obtain this last reform it had
to sacrifice one equally important which the
country had previously gained. Therefore,
it should think seriously about putting the
elective-Government idea first on its pro-
gramme, and making this reform the sole
price of its support. When it is gained,
then everything else will be possible. As
things are now, the political machine moves
so slowly and so rustily, and the processes of
Party Government are such a complete essay
on the art of not being able to do things,
that progress is too slow to be worth men-
tioning.
Some More Loan Estimates.
In a recent issue The Bulletin took occasion
to discourse on “ the Borrowing Boom ” in Aus-
tralia, and particularly in N.S. Wales, and
urged the necessity of defining by Act of Par-
liament what kind of works should be charged
to loan-moneys and what should be charged to
revenue. It advocates this legal definition for
several reasons more or less effective. It will
draw the attention of the country to the
matter ; the Loan Estimates go through so un-
obtrusively that very few people are really
aware how the thing is worked. It will also
draw the attention of the people who lend the
money, and thereafter they will lend it with
their eyes open—or, if they don’t, the fault
will be their own. It will put all Treasurers on
a level, whereas, as matters now stand, the dis-
honest Treasurer who makes a surplus by using
borrowed money for road-repairs and all manner
of other ordinary expenditure is a heaven-born
financier, while the honest one who doesn’t is
driven out of office, either for having a deficit
or for increasing taxation, and is a failure either
way. It is generally believed by the commu-
nity that New South Wales borrows money for
reproductive works only—that is, for works
which yield an actual cash revenue to the
Treasury. The following list shows what kind
of works Parliament has authorised to be con-
structed with borrowed money during the past
twelve years, and damages this public impres
sion rather severely :
Non-revenue-pro- Revenue-producing
dueing- works. works
ioqr "• £562,603 ... £13,825,700
4 ®®® ••• 161,040 ... 2,954,353

IQQQ - 297 > 487 - 1,117,081

1894 ... 665,925 ... 1,024 737
1895 ... 924,275 ... ’flS
* * * *
Ihe date put against each item is the vear in
which the expenditure was authorised by Par-
liament. Many of these works are not vet
finished ; some of them, in fact, are hardly
commenced ; but they have all been dulv sanc-
tioned, and most of them have been completed,
or are very nearly completed. Those which are
still in suspense include productive and unpro-
ductive works indiscriminately, so the money
actually spent is probably divided between the
two classes in about the same proportions as
shown above. The figures are taken from
the loan accounts just issued by the
N.S.W. Government. The list might have
been carried back many years further, but 1884
was chosen for a point of commencement for
several reasons. One was that, though the
fraud complained of has existed, more or less,
from the beginning of the borrowing system, it
was only after 1884 that it developed to any
serious extent. Another was that the remote
past is dead, and the people who swindled the
country then are mostly dead also, and there is
no one of any importance left alive to call to
account for the misdeeds of these days.
* * * *
The above figures show, with tolerable clear-
ness, why New South Wales has a surplus one
year and a deficit the next. In 1885, for
example, no loan was authorised, therefore
there was no chance to charge the ordinary
road and bridge expenditure of that year to
borrowed money, and a big deficit resulted. In
1886 the amount of the ordinary expenditure
thus “faked” was very small and another
deficit resulted. In 1887, again, no loan was
authorised, and the road and bridge and
other casual requirements of that year
couldn’t conveniently be passed on to pos-
terity, so presumably they came out of revenue,
and some more big deficit naturally ensued.
In 1888, however, a great and patriotic Govern-
ment arose and squared the national balance-
sheet, and produced a surplus of half-a-million.
That was the year when nearly £1,500,000 of
what should have been current expenditure for
bridges, gaols, dredging works, repairs to streets
and wharves, gunpowder and other military
stores, and the like, was shoved unobtrusively
into a new loan, to the great relief of the
revenue. In 1889 the same Government had a
deficit of £lBl,OOO, the simple and obvious
explanation being that the amount paid out of
loans for road-making, wood-blocking of streets,
bridges, gaols, and miscellaneous jobs and
repairs was only about .£l,loo,ooo—consider-
ably less than in the great surplus year of 1888.
In 1890 there was another small surplus, and
the charging of nearly .£1,200,000 of ordinary
expenditure to loans at least partly explains its
existence. In 1891-2-3 the loan money swindle
was almost extinct for the time being—possibly
because loans were almost unobtainable just
then; but by heavy increases of taxation a
much-execrated Treasurer managed to get
through with a small surplus and two moderate
deficits. The reason of the alleged financial
prosperity of ’94 and ’95 is partly to be found
in the “ cash basis ” system of accountancy,
and partly in the figures shown above. This
particular loan swindle isn’t the whole reason
of the province’s financial fluctuations, but it
is one reason, and the principal one.
• * » •
Very few people —probably very few politi-
cians even—fully understand the wild humor of
the loan-mongering business in New South
Wales. By far the largest proportion of last
year’s Public Works loan was for building
bridges, repairing old wooden bridges, making
roads, wood-blocking streets, thinning out-
forests, constructing gaols, lunatic asylums and
other works which yield nothing whatever
towards paying interest on their cost. And
some of these bridges that are now built with
borrowed money are to replace old, worn-out
bridges which have not yet been paid for, and
some of these latter probably replaced still
older ones for which the bill is still outstand-
ing. The original wharf at the foot of Erskine-
street, Sydney, was built with borrowed money
in ’56, and when it fell to pieces about ’9o—
Bulletin can’t trace any other unpaid-for
wharf in between, but there may have been one
—another was built, also with borrowed money,
and it will probably have to be shortly re-
moved in order to make room for a big steam-
boat wharf built with borrowed money also.
Yet the bill for that old wharf of ’55 is as far
from being liquidated as ever it was, or as
the bill for the 1890 one now is. The
roadway at Circular Quay, Sydney, has
been reconstructed at least three times and
charged to loans every time. Almost all the
old locomotives which have worn themselves
into scrap-iron, and the old railway carriages
which have become matchwood, and the old
trucks which have gone to dust since the rail-
ways first began, are not paid for even now.
The original Sydney General Post Office was
built with borrowed money, and the cost of
pulling it down was defrayed out of borrowed
money, and the present one was built with
borrowed money, and all these bills are still
outstanding, and will probably be outstanding
when the next G.P.O. is wanted. The first
Sydney water-supply, which has been long since
superseded, is an unpaid debt even now.
There is hardly in all the province a closed and
abandoned road, or a worn-out punt or dredge,
or an obsolete telegraph instrument, orasilted-
up river, or the memory of a wharf which fell
to pieces 20 years ago, which doesn’t represent
some part of the “ reproductive and permanent”
assets which are supposed to be good security for
the public debt of Botany Bay.
* * * *
Still, though the great Public Works swindle
is very old, it is only within the last ten or
twelve years that it assumed serious dimensions
and became the permanent, reliable swindle it
now is. Also, it is only within the last year


that non-productive jobs began to represent the
largest part of the loan-expenditure, and when
forest-thinning became one of the recognised
“ public works.” N.S. Wales now spends some
£600,000 a year of borrowed money on bridges
and buildings and other ephemeral enterprises
which yield no revenue. And it must
keep on building bridges, &c., at this rate
for probably another 100 years before the
province is fairly opened up, and every
year the interest on the unproductive debt
grows more and more, till at last it alone
will amount to probably <£1,000,000 per annum ;
and, as the bridges and other things multiply,
the time must come when another ,£300,000 or
£400,000 or £500,000 a year will have to be
borrowed for repairs, and before the great
work is finished it will be necessary to start
borrowing to build them all over again, because
old age and decrepitude will have come upon
them ; and so on ad infinitum. Consequently,
The Bulletin suggests that the present is as
good a time as any to stop short and think seri-
ously over the whole matter. Any time in the
last ten years, or even in the last 40 years,
would have done as well ; but those times
are past and the present one is still with us.
What this paper suggests is a short Act of
Parliament defining how far the present
system is to go ; and stating exactly whether
it is to stop at forest-thinning or go on to
include the destitute aboriginal’s blanket and
the clothes of the old man in the Benevolent
Asylum as well as the building he lives in ; and
explaining how the crumbling wooden wharf,
and the river that is dredged to-day and silted
up again to-morrow, come to be described as
permanent works ; and otherwise putting the
matter on a definite basis. It isn’t at all neces-
sary for Parliament to stop the present system
if it doesn’t feel so inclined, but it might as
well define the system, if only in order that it
can’t profess afterwards not to have known
what was going on.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Liquor Anomaly.
According to Victorian law, any retailer found
vending liquor not up to the standard strength,
or falsely labelled, or otherwise unwholesome,
bogus or adulterated, is heavily fined —nomin-
ally in the interests of the national stomach.
It may be proved quite clearly that he couldn’t
have tampered with the liquor, and had no
chance of knowing what it was like, it being
bottled, corked and sealed in the original pack-
ages, and it may be perfectly clear that he
didn't tamper with it—but the law holds him
responsible none the less because it was in his
possession. The other day a Melbourne publi-
can suspected that some liquor he had bought
was not what it professed to be, and called in
an excise officer to inspect it. The inspection
proved it to be a nauseous and adulterated
compound, and the retail groggist, as the result
of his own conscientiousness, was heavily fined
for having bad whisky on his premises. In his
defence he pleaded that he didn’t adulterate the
liquor, which seemed more than probable seeing
that it was he who called attention to it and
caused its confiscation ; and that it was exactly
as the wholesaler supplied it to him ; but he
was fined none the less. The situation as
between the wholesale and retail grog-dealer in
Victoria is a quaint affair, and stands practi-
cally as follows : (1) The retail dealer gener-
ally runs a “tied” house, which means that he
has to get his supplies. from one particular
wholesaler who holds a mortgage over him—-
therefore that wholesaler may send him what-
ever rubbish he pleases ; (2) The police con-
stantly inspect the retailer’s liquor but not the
wholesale dealer’s, therefore it is always the
former who falls in ; (3) when the retail dealer
says that his bad liquor is just as he received it,
and the wholesale person denies the statement,
as he always does, the law always believes the
wholesale witness. The law requires the pub-
lican to absolutely prove his statement, and the
only way it can be proved is by searching the
wholesale man’s premises and finding the evi-
dence there in the shape of the bad liquor
itself ; and the police never overhaul the big
man’s premises as they do the retailer’s, or pay
him surprise visits, or keep an effective eye on
him in any way. Therefore, while the law in-
sists that the retailer shall prove his statements
it, at the same time, takes all manner of care
that, he shall not be able to prove them. (4)
The law regards the wholesale dealer and the
retailer from entirely different standpoints.
If two moderately-reputable witnesses produce
a sample of bad liquor and swear that they
bought it from a certain publican, it is ten to
one that he is fined regardless of his denials ;
whereas The Bulletin never heard of a whole-
sale dealer being fined, no matter what the evi-
dence might be, so long as he simply denied the
charge. The law’s reverence for and confidence
in the wholesale grog-merchant is a strange
and wonderful emotion. :(5) The law which
does these remarkable things was created and
is maintained by a Parliament in which large,
affluent wholesale grog-dealers are numerous
and retailers are almost, if not entirely, absent.
Therefore, Victorian legal practice is based on
the great axiom that the wdiolesale grog-dealer
can do no wrong.
The Celtic Myth.
Dear Bulletin, —In between the war-whoops
of the indignant Caledonians, who will, doubt-
less, ere this have started out for your Philo-
Celtic correspondent’s scalp, let me, who am
neither Scotch nor “ scotty,” inquire of that
gentle commentator where, amongst British
poets, the Celt has “ let his soul go forth into
the night ” to any overpowering extent. Take
the six greatest names for imagination and
emotion. Shakespeare, Wordsworth, and
Keats are Saxon ; Milton, Shelley, and
Gordon (Byron) are Norman —as are Chaucer,
Spenser, and Tennyson ; while Dryden, Pope,.
and Gray, again, are Saxon. The Celt, we
are always being told—principally by him-
self—is poetically of the first heroic water,,
when he isn’t of the finest anacreontic. For
the first, I am assured—likewise mostly by
himself—that in his various original vernaculars
he was herein unsurpassed and unsurpassable.
A poor dozen of centuries ago, this, and I seek
not to dispel the enchantment attendant as
much upon durational distance as upon physical.
But —bar the ingenious manufacturer of the
supposed Ossianic dithyrambs —upon what
modern Celt has descended any appreciable
corner of this semi-mythic mantle of heroic
poesy? Where, too, be the Celts’ anacreontics?
Dependent for a collective reputation upon the
tin-pot tinklings of little Tommy Moore !
Alas, poor Celt ! Great may be the Burns
myth, but greater is the Celtic! — Cold Water.
Tlie Dean Curse.
“Dead, Fred. Curnow, secretary to the Dean -
Commission.” This is the fortunate young man
whom the other day all his fellow-scribes were
envying for his good luck in being son of editor
Curnow, of S.M. Herald, and near relation
of Mr. Brunker, N.S.W. Chief Secretary,
and in getting “ more than the Chief Justice’s,
salary ” in fees as attache to various Govt.
Commissions, whereof that on the Dean case
came last. The matter of Mr. Curnow’s
fees, indeed, caused quite a public scandal,
though Curnow, of course, only took what
he was permitted to take, and what his-
envious trade-rivals would gladly have taken
themselves had they got the chance. And now
Curnow is dead —dead of a fly-bite, a nothing—
a cold which he caught a fortnight ago. Elwell,
his highly-fee’d coadjutor in the business of
“ taking down ” the history of the Deans, is
already underground. It gives pause to the
Man in the Street. “ The Dean curse again !
where is It going to stop ? ” The Dean curse
will never stop. The Dean curse will go on to
eternity, as long as there is a single human
being left to work upon. For the Dean curse
is just one letter of one page of one volume of
the infinite edition of curses which afflict
humanity. The Dean curse ! —why, it
isn’t an iota, it isn’t a fragment, it is hardly
a grain of dust on the whiskers of the
one great Curse that sums up all the other
curses—the Curse of Existence. W e only see
the Dean curse at all by dint of concentrating
public attention upon it: the Man-in-the-Street.
has his telescope focussed on Dean all the time,
and that’s why he imagines a coincidence in the
series of disasters that have befallen actors in the.
grim Dean tragedy. But consider any set of
individuals, any section of society, with the
same concentrated glare, and note how people
everywhere topple over into agony or oblivion
as fast as those who essayed to walk the
bridge in the Vision of Mirza. '' by,
for nine-tenths of its inhabitants, the
world is only a torture-chamber, a hospital,
an asylum, or a slaughter-house in which some-
times, as an act of rarest grace, you are per-
mitted to choose your executioner ! lake
vour own friends —the mates you drank and
laughed with a dozen, even half-a-dozen, years
ago. Where are they now ? Brown dead last
year of cancer : he “never could see much use
in legislation.” Jones —you watched him in
the street yesterday, a wreck and a cripple,
with the face of a Methuselah every one of
whose 969 years had been spent in a, hell
of pain. Robinson — he took liis friends
wife : only one thing that means. Smith— -his
third insolvency was advertised this morning.
Thompson —gaol. Johnson —asylum. Harri-
son—poor fellow ! his lot was worst of all
they made him member of aGoD-fearing Minis-
try. And so you may go on : happy to draw
one honor in the whole pack. Fact is, every
man to whom Fate gives a little rope gets the
habit of counting up all his puny pleasures and
looking across them at things in geneial;
while it takes a »mighty lot of other-fel-
lows’ misfortunes to give him a mighty little
impression. By-and-by the Big thumb comes
along and flattens him out, and then, if he gets
a bit of writliing-space before the Great Dark
swallows him up, he begins to sniff Life s
charnel-house. That’s so. Yet what s the use
of crying about it ?
This Life is a game that is played, and this World but a
booth in a show : ,
Cast earnestness off then, and play up, or sit down anu
be mute with your
Item from ex-Treasurer M‘Millan’s blasting
indictment of Premier Reid’s Budget and
alleged surplus :
In the year 1889 Mr. M ‘Mill an- expended £182,210 more
than he got: in the year 1890 there was a cash deficiency
of £51,266, and in the year 1891 there was a deficiency of
£331,450. Now he came to the two years when Mr. See
held office, and here he thought that Mr. Reid would have
done better had he been perfectly fair and candid. It did
not matter whether a Government were Protectionist or
Freetrade if they were honest and economical. I he posi-
tion of accounts in 1892 was this : The cash deficiency was
£35,716, the lowest cash deficiency up to this time. Next
year (1893), however, the deficiency was £582,288, and m
the year 1894, during half of which Mr. Reid was in office,
the deficiency was £29,242. In the first six months of
1895 Mr. Reid had a cash deficiency of £366,028, this being
at the rate of £720,000 a year. In the financial year 1895-
96, and it did not matter whether they covered it up
with Treasury bills or anything else as the country would
have to pay the piper, there was a cash deficiency of
£634,169 for the 12 months. There could be no dispute
about these figures, and it did not matter what had been
done with Treasury bills. During his own Treasurership
the aggregate deficiency was £564,926 between expendi-
ture and money received. The average annual deficiency
of his administration was £180,308. Then the aggregate
for Mr. See’s two and a half years was £796,076, or an
average per annum of £318,430. The deficiency on the
part of Mr. Reid's Administration for the two years was
£851,483, or an average of £425,000.
A Cheeky Minority.
The Maoriland Bible-in-schools party made its
usual regular appearance this session, and its
bill was thrown out by 43 votes against 11.
The one thing on which these provinces appear
to be solid all round is that they don’t want to
mix up Scripture and education, or to introduce
parsonical influence into the schools in any
form whatever. Yet though the Scriptural
party is utterly licked wherever it goes to a
vote, it keeps on asserting, with a gall that
passeth all understanding, that the great mass
of the people are really with it. In S.A., after
being hopelessly extinguished on many occa-
sions in the Legislature, it persistently alleged
that all it wanted was a straight vote of the
whole people, and when it got this it would
wipe its opponents off the face of the earth.
At last election it got the referendum it had
bowled for so long and agonisingly. Three
questions were submitted: (1) “Are you in
favor of maintaining the present secular
system ? ” on which the Bible-party was
■defeated by 51,000 votes against 17,000 ;
i(2) “ Are you in favor of Scriptural in-
struction in schools?” where the Scripture-
party was licked by 34,000 votes against 19,000;
and (3) “ Are you in favor of State assistance
to denominational schools?” on which point the
pious party went down by 42,000 votes against
13,000. The majority was so tremendous on
■every point that for a few weeks the Scripture
■crowd kept silence and didn’t even attempt to
■explain things away—in fact, it seemed as if no
■one could explain away such majorities as these.
But now it has got its second wind, and once
more a voice like a deacon crying in the wilder-
ness asserts that the mass of the people wants
the Bible-in-schools system, and wants it badly,
and that the referendum was all a mistake.
Most of the voters answered only the first ques-
tion on the voting-paper, because it really
■covered everything ; and some answered the
■second and third only. So a section of the
Scriptural party now rises on two argumentative
legs to assert that the people who didn’t vote on
■any one or two questions were necessarily
favorable to the church crowd on these ques-
tions, and if they were all added together, as
they ought to he, and credited to the church,
as they should be, it would have a big majority.
On this principle, if one man voted straight for
a continuance of the present system, and left
the other two questions unanswered because
the first one really covered the whole ground,
be would count as one vole for the cause lie
voted for and two votes for the cause he voted
against. A party which wants to count the
ballots this way is a very hard case to deal
with, and can fairly claim to be the cheekiest
little minority in this continent.
Ed. Bulletin,— The sooner Premier Reid, and
the parties who superintend the relief to the
■destitute, think out some means of doing away
with the hideous spectacle to be seen on Thurs-
days at Sydney Benevolent Asylum, when
■Society throws its scraps to those who are
whipped up by necessity to the place of degrada-
tion”, the better for all concerned. It would be
kinder to the great majority of the pensioners
! xo quietly shoot them than continue the present
■caricature of benevolence. To serve out
| groceries, clothes, and donations towards the
rent is a gigantic blunder, for toilers are easily
converted into paupers, and the present relie
system must inevitably reduce its victims to a
state similar to that of the emancipated W est
Indian negroes. The object of all relief organi-
sations, private or national, should he to put tne
recipient of their aid in a position to maintain
himself and family; the Government should
classify the unemployed and put those who can
and will work on to reproductive works till they
can earn enough to make a fresh start in hie
the remainder, who cannot and will not woi
should be provided for in asylums and gaols.
Work, hospital or gaol : those are the three
sound alternatives. Last Thursday the wnter
made enquiries among a large number of men
waiting at the so-called Benevolent Asylum,
and numbers of them admitted they had been
receiving State aid for two, three and four
years. When a man has been living on State
charity for four or even two years it proves
that either the man is useless or the State is an
economic imbecile. To a man and wife of the
unemployed section the State-subsidised society
issues weekly 4 loaves, 21b. flour, 21b. meat, 4 lb.
tea, and lib. of coarse brown sugar, and, when
found necessary, blankets and boots. 11ns is
continued week after week till the man
obtains work, commits suicide, or the wife
becomes immoral, when the relief is stoppei .
Judging from the remarks and conversation ol
the crowd, a number of the wives do beconu
immoral, and until New South Wales realises
that its present alleged benevolent system of
relief is wrong in theory and unutterably woise
in practice, the wives will go on getting moie
and more immoral, and the men more and more
useless, and the unemployed larger and larger
till the community will either fall to pieces or
do something rash.—Z.
N.S.W. Opposition-leader Lyne is reported a,B
saying, in a recent speech at Windsor, that il
he came into power he “ did not say he would
retain the land and income tax.” Presumably
this means that he wouldn’t retain it, and, if
so, Lyne’s usual big mistake sticks out con-
spicuously. When will some influential politi-
cian arise who will tell the country the naked
truth about its position ? Said truth is that
Protection isn’t a means of creating revenue
(except incidentally and in its earlier stages),
but a means of creating wealth and building up
industries and furnishing employment. Also,
that the ordinary expenditure of N.S. Wales,
even on a very cheap basis, is about <£10,500,000
a year, but £1,000,000 to £1,500,000 of
it is surreptitiously charged to loans to
create a bogus aspect of economy ; and to meet
this the Treasury wants all the Land and
Income tax it has,' and possibly more, and all it
can get incidentally from a Protective tariff as
well. Further, that if tne Government raised
£1,500,000 a year more taxation than it does,
and spent it on profitable works, it would do
vastly more to relieve distress than by borrow-
ing or by stopping public works to keep down
the figures of the budget. The wail every where
is that distress is caused by gold lying idle in
banks instead of being employed in useful
undertakings. AGovernment which had courage
enough to annex a lot of that unemployed gold
and start useful undertakings with it, and to
tell the people that what they u anted was more
taxation, provided the money was profitably
employed, instead of less, would be a blessing
to this land of the Fool’s Paradise.
One day lately four men plea led guilty to
travelling on N.S.W. railway lines without
tickets. On the same day a woman at Newtown
pleaded guilty to sending her boy to steal coal
from the State-railway shed. In each case the
accused indicated that they thought it a less
crime to steal public property than private.
This represents the percolation through the
body civil of the ages-old politician brand of
morality. The politician scorns to help himself
or his family out of Tom Smith’s grocery or
Bill Jones’ calico emporium, but he puts his
sister-in-law in a £2OO a year Government
billet or thrusts a sap-headed son into the door-
way of some State Department, and helps the
boy to spend the produce on board and lodging
in the house of dad.
Charles Dangar, a Surry Hills illicit whisky-
maker, was fined £5OO the other day in a
Sydney Court. The alternative punishment
was a year’s gaol. Just think of the illicit
whisky-maker of the present time paying a fine
of £5OO ! And, if he had the £5OO, the Court
is certainly not going to get it. I irst, there is
no big man now in the nefarious trade; or, if
there be, he only lends a trifle of cash at mas-
sive interest, and is able to absolutely defy the
law. The men now running stills about Sydney
are but little above the unemployed level, so
far as wealth is concerned. They are robbed
at everv point. They must, bribe at every
point. And when they are caught they are just
as poor as when they started. And £5OO or a
year in gaol! If the law made it £5O or two
years, most of those captured would have to
take the two years.
The N.S.W. kindergarten movement, so long
as it is under the patronage of fashionables
and faddists, promises to become another ex-
crescence on the education-system of thecountrj.
Froebel’s idea is to teach by play ; but play is
healthy and invigorating only when it is free and
spontaneous, and the kindergarten, “bossed”
by any broken-down governess who can do the
requisite amount of mechanical stuffing with
the “groups” of toys, will but add another
horror to child-life in our great cities. The
kindergarten has a distinct psychological value,
and every modern teacher worthy of the name
apprehends that his methods must be rooted
firmly in psychological laws —but it is pe-
culiarly a system that requires for its
sound application the genius of the born
teacher—precisely the quality a committee of
fashionable faddists cannot detect. When,
the other day, Gov. Hampden told a Sydney
audience he knew little about Froebel’s
system, he possibly suggested to the pro-
moters of he gathering that w.th such an
aristocratic example of nescience before them
they could well afford to be equally candid.
Reading of the troubles, feuds, and tribu-
lations of Melb. Secular Society. How these
Secularists love one another !
Heaton on Parkes.
The most awful obituary reference to Parbes
was that published in London by Henniker
Heaton. Such greatness as the old man had
has heen pretty well discounted by the fulsome
falsehoods of his “ friends,” but the meanest of
all tributes have sprung (as in the nature of the
case was to be expected) from those who bat-
tened parasitically on his fame. Listen to
Heaton or his “ ghost ” :
He (Parkes) adhered rigidly to his own standard of
right and wrong, and no earthly inducement could draw
hnn an inch to the right or left from it.
Parses, in later years, developed too essenti-
ally a feminine nature to have moral criteria.
Up to the moment of the Empire cataclysm,
the man’s virility had, at least, equal play to
his femininity ; after that event, the other side
of him took control. His inordinate vanity,
his craving for a retinue of women and for the
perpetual incense of her-parasites among men,
his incapacity to be just before being generous,
his subservience to the impulse of the moment,
his impatience of detail, his love of the orotund,
were all feminine characteristics, and in them-
selves symptomatic of the repugnance of the
feminine order of mind to create moral stan-
dards for itself. Like Coleridge, Parkes had
“no morals.”
The surest way to prevent him from granting a public
favor was to do him a private favor.
An absolutely indisputable utterance —if
Parkes’s 1.0. U. Ministries, Executive Comis-
sioners’ appointments, and fifty other co-
ordinate facts can be blown out of existence by
Heaton’s perfumed breath.
He had no vices; he neither smoked, nor drank, nor
gambled ; he hated idleness; his whole time was so
sacredly devoted to the public service that he hesitated
even to speak with persons unknown to him, his theory
being that it was sheer waste of time to converse with
nine men out of ten.
The verbal statement of this “ theory.” Parkes
prigged from a Palmerstonian remark, and
in his practice he set it at defiance daily.
No public man, in or out of office, was more
easily accessible to the idling stranger and
friendly loafer who come to flatter. His time
was perpetually occupied, it is true ; but it was
usually with the business of a fribble. No man
had a finer faculty of deceiving himself with
the notion that the trifles that engaged his
attention were important. He always remained
a toy-maker—and played with his toys when
he should have been working. Heaton, pace
ghost, again :
“ I remember his paying away all the little money he
could command for a holographic letter of Oliver Crom-
well’s which I had discovered.”
The generosity which prompts the reminiscence
might well have been taxed to save the old
man’s “ little money ” by a donation of the pre-
cious holograph “discovered” —in the nearest
bookseller’s —by Mr. Henniker Heaton.
“ Sir Henry’s utterances were to ordinary ears dis-
figured by a careless use of the aspirate.”
“ A superior critic this, my Lord ! ” But what
of “ extraordinary ears ” such as doubtless con-
stitute “ the aural appendages ” —sweet Even-
ing News phrase, this !—of Heaton’s “ghost”?
“We all remember the immortal phrase in which he
expressed the Imperial idea —‘ the crimson thread of
kinship’—that ran throughout the Empire.”
Mr. Heaton’s “ghost’s” grammar is shaky
somewhat. What ran throughout the Empire
—the phrase, the idea, or the “crimson thread”?
Anyhow, the phrase wasn’t Parkes’. But,
for the Heatonian finale :
I am aware that he has been heavily censured, almost
ostracised, for marrying . . . Without entering into
the abstract questions of morality involved, I would
merely observe that in this, as in every other action, he
followed the dictates of a singularly uncompromising
conscience.
The Bulletin does not know which to admire
most: the humor or the good taste of Mr.
Heaton’s “ ghost.” In the latter quality he
can boast close comparison with the Sydney
daily which, on the morning succeeding Parkes’
death, gave the date of his second marriage,
and, immediately below this intelligence, the
ages of the children of the second Lady Parkes.
“ A singularly uncompromising conscience.”
The hunror of it !

The present marvellously-successful Syd-
ney Lyceum show ceases from troubling after
baturday night, and for nearly a week
the theatre will be deserted. On the ensu-
es Saturday (August 15) Mrs. Potter and
Ayrle Bellew appear in “La Tosca,” with the
lovely Cora as the heroine, and Kyrle in the
dark and shuddersome character of Baron Scarpia.
Ihe Bulletin has hopes that the interval will be
made use of to clean the wall-paper up aloft,
where the grease-mark of the heads of many gods
is painfully conspicuous.

Rev. Charles Clark is doing his last series
of lectures at Sydney Centenary Hall, and a few
stray cycloramas and other trifles are filling in the
great void to the best of their ability. Meanwhile,
Her Majesty s and the Criterion keep shut with
an energy that is worthy of a better cause, and
two or three ancient places of amusement which
nave been closed for ages have suddenly remained
that way with great success.
** # *
The Frantz Family is still engaged making
a lean and much afflicted city sit up in wonder-
ment Also Provo, the amazing juggler, has re-
turned. When one considers Provo in all his
glory, and how he can bring something out of
nothing, and rest things on top of things that
aren t there, and make large articles go away of
their own accord, and make impossible things
happen and make other things impossibly stop
happening, and how he can make circumstances
he going to occur last year and cause them to have
occurred next week, this paper really begins to
understand all about the financial statement for
the first time. And when Provo explains how
these things are done in a way that makes them
more obscure than ever, The Bulletin gets the
uu n s- J of M Malian's comments on Reid in a way
that it never did before.

** * *
The cinematographe ”is an evolution of
Mr. Edison’s “ kinetoscope,” by M. Lumiere, of
.Lyons, the celebrated inventor of the “ dry plate.”
A series of photographs are taken so raoidly that
they practically represent every movement of the
living or moving objects before the lens, as the
human eye cannot distinguish an action which re-
quires less than a tenth of a second to complete
its motion. In each picture there are 900 nega-
tives and the same machine that takes them serves
to lenect them (nearly life-size) on a screen. A
cinematographe for Sydney is aboard the incoming
Uroya, and a second machine is to follow. The
introducer is confident of a boom.
** # *
nt “ l, n Mizzoura,” the second attempt of the
Nat Goodwin Co. at Melb. Princess’, is a calm
pine-log drama of domestic life way out West or
bouth- West, where everybody, bar an occasional
accident, is real grit for dead sartin, and the
noblest man m the crowd always spits with the
greatest deliberation. Australia got an agreeable
taste of In Mizzoura ” from “ The Countv Fair ”
an importation of some few years ago, which was
brought into disrepute by an absurd mechanical
horse-race. There is nothing ridiculous about the
present drama. It relates to human existence in
a blacksmith s cottage, and derives its simple
interest from real 'Murkan dialect and ap-
propriate surroundings. It is a drama of
magnificent distances, or pauses, between
laconic remarks apropos to siiiall dailv events
aT ? c ! humble aspirations. It is a drama in
which the entire cast might be expected to collapse
at the news that the taters were overboiled Most
of the characters carry real grit on their faces as
well as inside ’em ; the one clean, town-made in-
terloper is an oasis of scoundrelism in a desert of
native simplicity, and the only woman who finds
the grimy social atmosphere oppressive is a poor
young victim to boarding-school education. This
superior Kate takes kindly to the evil interloper
because he doesn’t chew terbacker- or wear big
feet, and a laconic Jim (the prototype of his Aus
tralian namesake) shows his silent love for the girl
by lending her dude a horse wherein to flee from
justice.
.** * *
Jim is a self-sacrificer, possessed of a
genius for saying little at a slow pace, or leaning
silently against his thoughts. Nat Goodwin im-
personates Jim for all he is worth. It is a part to
be walked through, with folded arms and a ’Mur-
kan drawl, in the manner of -ToeJefferson, and the
star doubtless shines as much as any actor could
shine in such a character. The bulk of his fine
manly feeling is left entirely to the imagination.
He travels on an unruffled demeanor like our old
friend Sass, who in evening-dress was as true to
life m the West of London as Jim is true to life in
the Western States. But this is not acting to
gush over. The parts, mostly amusing, of Bill,
Sam, Dave, ’Lizbeth, and the inevitable colonel
are also naturally represented. The fact that all
these restful rustics talk the same dialect in a
stereotyped tone has a certain monotonous
charm, very acceptable for a change, though
not calculated to grow upon urban audiences.
Jo, the blacksmith, appears rather unnatu-
rally monotonous, but Miss Mortimer, as his
wife, gives a brisk motherly performance. Miss
Blanche Walsh, who was sweetly subdued in “ A
Gilded Fool,” is now sweetly melodramatic in the
Kate character, her impassioned raising of a storm
in the cottage tea-cup being, perhaps, intended to
warn blacksmiths against educating their eldest
daughters. “In Mizzoura ” is billed to glorify
Jim for one week only. On Saturday (Bth) Nat
Goodwin will produce further proof of his versa-
tility in “The Rivals,” one of the most un-
American comedies ever written.
** * *
The next special feature at the Opera House
will be Carl Hertz, who takes the place of Charles
Godfrey and brings some new illusions that are
said to be even more ingenious than his first lot.
Hertz starts on Saturday. Likewise the Bijou
will open again on Saturday, this time under the
management of Cosgrove minus Marie Luella, with
A Trip to Chicago.”
** * *
“ Cheer Boys Cheer,” the novelty unloosed
by George Rignold at Melb. Royal, is a stupen-
dous entertainment, in five massive acts. The last
massive act didn’t begin until 11.30 p.m. on Satur-
day, and when The Bulletin chronicler tore him-
self away to catch a midnight train, it was from a
scene of gilded splendor, wherein a tall flunkey
stood announcing members of Hingland’s alleged
aristocracy as they came to hand. A sassiety
denouement seemed likely to happen soon, but the'
railway time-table was inexorably fixed. Thirty-
seven people, mostly of proud lineage, figure on
the programme, and, in addition to these,
there are two small detachments of British troops
and a band of very black Matabele braves who
make a record by overwhelming George Rignold
half-an-hour before closing-time. George falls
mortally wounded whilst singing “ God Save the
CJueen, the death of that hitherto-invincible hero
being the chief surprise in the show. He expires
upon a heap of slain after an informal marriage
with the girl he had misjudged, the band playing
Chopins Marche Funebre.as a guarantee of
burial to come. Manager Rignold and his drama
moved too slowly on Saturday night, yet, con-
sidering the vast dimensions of the undertaking,
the first performance was a successful trial.
* * * *
Cheer Boys Cheer ” has humor and
satire m its various comedy sketches, as well as a
reasonable plot. The polished villain is a cheerful
schemer, and his clerk—an amateur demagogue
who waves a red signal for the masses to revolt—
seems bound to become popular. A designing
lady s-maid contributes a few jokes; also, numer-
ous other people say, or do, things that bear upon
the awfulness of modern civilisation and leave an .
impression by the way. In fact, the drama,
although overloaded with spectacular effects and
unnecessary expenses, has claims to be called a
playu In the matter of all-round opportunities for
smart acting “Cheer Boys Cheer” stands alone
among shows built specially for Drury Lane
Iheatre.
* * * *
The company engaged by G.R. ranges
from very good to middling and worse. Miss
Lmily Hughes, fresh from ’Ome, is an attractive
little lady, who bears herself daintily under diffi-
cult circumstances in the bad young man’s private
sitting-room, and acts with pretty vocal and facial
expression throughout the trouble. Her best
points would be more telling in a smaller house
than the Royal, nevertheless she took her place
there with capital results. Plimmer, as the
abnormally cool villain, is another bright spot,
and Haiis Phillips plays the clerk well enough
. ™ake himself a favorite. Miss Watts-
I lumps turns the lady’s-maid character to fair
account; Sweeney repeats his previous caricature
ot the greasy Hebrew that is said to libel an
ancient race ; and Miss Maud Williamson scores in
a light, u indefinable capacity. Stirling White and
Miss Leigh, as a pair of sinful pietists, are mild
artistic failures in comparison with J. G. Patten
who meets his liabilities to the extent of about
twopence in the £. A dozen minor performers
can scramble for half-a-dozen words of negative
praise and the same number of unpleasant remarks.
John Brunton has painted an abundance of fine
scenery for “Cheer Boys Cheer,” but the stage-
management on the first night was not up to Rig-
nold s standard. The show should improve rapidlv
and prosper.

lassies are as keen on this sort of thing as their

he s burning already ! ”

Dr. Neild, who presented Maggie Moore
with her purse of sovs. and complimentary address
the other night, is the best of showmen for a little
job like that. At one fell swoop he knocked 22
years off the heroine’s age by calling her Maggie
m brisk, fatherly style, and referring to her Aus-
tralian debut as quite a recent event in his memory.
“Maggie” seemed to be back again in the old
times as the doctor talked, and her unaffected
returning of thanks, coming between the doctor’s
speech and a few more remarks by George Coppin,
was about the most youthful performance she has
accomplished on the boards of Melbourne Roval.

A very poor house on the second night of
“ A Gilded Fool ” alarmed Nat. Goodwin unduly.
Announcements that his opening piece would run
a fortnight, with a matinee thrown in, were im-
mediately modified. ’ “A Gilded Fool” would
have to be withdrawn at the end of the week in
consequence, &c., &c., and the matinee idea was
dropped overboard. Then the general public
started rolling up quietly to take a “liker” at
Nathaniel and his new show from America, and
when the sixth night’s takings came to hand the
management felt sorry for having so hastily aban-
doned the original plan of procedure. Business
had been on the up-grade since the second occasion,
which is notoriously an unsafe “test night ’ to go
upon. The Firm had a really astonishing re-
minder of this fact with “Gentleman Joe” last
year, when the second night’s receipts were
doubled, or thereabouts, on the Tuesday.

The very “ Berritish ” Melb. Age slated
Miss Ethel Browning, in “A Gilded Fool,” be-
cause of her Americanisms and her Yankee twang.
Goodwin, the low comedian, and Miss Browning
playing a vivacious young Yank, are the only two
who indulge in dialect, and the paper, which
quotes Pinero as an ideal, piles on its dignity and
whoops for orthoepical purity. Yet if a British
playwright uses a Yorkshireman, or a Lanky, or
a Cockney, with his hideous dialect thick upon
him, who would object and accuse the actor and
the author of diabolically defiling the pure well of
English ? The American dialect is ali ving thing,
it is of the people, and stripped of their twang
and their peculiar idioms these men and women
would not receive adequate representation at the
hands of an actor or an actress. In “A Gilded
Fool ” Miss Browning and Goodwin handle char-
ters who would probably talk dialect. Most
edifying it is to hear two-thirds of John Bull
abusing the American for his dialect in a language
that is mere gibberish to the majority of the Eng-
lish-speaking peoples. The mote in the other
fellow s mouth and the beam in his own.

Rossi, the Italian tragedian, died of a chill caught at
bt. Petersburg, three months before his actual decease.
He left the theatre in an open sleigh, one icy night, for
the sake of showing himself to the roaring populace, and
never recovered from the ill effects of his triumphal drive.

Ex-hangman Berry was plaintiff, the other week, in an
amusing action against a London music-hall proprietor.
Berry claimed a balance of weekly salary for lecturing on
On im mils I have Met”— i.e., subjects he has “worked
off, seeing that he never met them away from the scaffold
or condemned cell. The defence alleged that the grue-
some artist came up drunk on the last night of his en-
gagement too drunk to lecture —and was, therefore,
ejected from the premises. To which Berry replied that
it was quite usual for the manager to accuse artists of
insobriety, in order to avoid paying. The two policemen
| a< been called upon to remove the gentleman swore
-v w . as as sober as any judge who ever gave him a
job, and finally the diplomatic defendant was ordered to
pay, with costs.
