The Wild-Cat Column .
The N.B.W. Budget compares rather badly with
that of Victoria, despite the outward appearance of
things. Reid had a surplus of ±330,000 ; Turner a net
deficiency estimated at fully ±60,000. On the other hand,
N.S.W. spent something like ±2,000,000 of loan-moneys,
and somewhere about half of this was for unproductive
works which ought fairly to have heen charged to revenue ;
Victoria spent ±219,000 of loan-moneys, and all of it was
on reproductive works. The N.S. VV. public-debt increased,
so far as appears at present, rather over ±1,500,000, and,
despite savings by conversions, the interest increases by
£15,000 ; the Victorian debt diminished ±303,000— though
against this there is an increase in the deficit, which is
charged temporarily against the trust funds—and the
interest payment is reduced by £12,000. Reid estimates
his next year’s deficit at ,£125,000 ; Turner anticipates a
shortage of ±79,000. Altogether, on close inspection,
most of the points seem to be in Turner’s favor.
* * * *
The =£25,000 spent last year in sand-shifting
relief works, at Sydney Centennial Park, wasn’t charged
to revenue. It was temporarily borrowed out of the trust
funds ; and matters were squared by writing up the value
of the Government property at the park by ±25,000,
making the paper-value ±224,371 !! t Another item in
the beautiful surplus. Such book-keeping would make a
cat laugh.
* * * *
Treasurer Nelson, of Queensland, reports a sur-
plus for the year of ±73,636, and, according to the Act
passed last year, this amount has to be handed over to the
trustees for the reduction of the public-debt. The beauty
and advantage of this system is that the alleged surplus
has to be produced in actual cash, and, as it goes out of
the Treasurer’s hands, he isn’t tempted by its existence
to go on the burst next year. There was a time when
N.S.W. had an accumulated surplus of ±3,889,000, but, as
it wasn’t put out of the Treasurer’s reach on the wise
principle established by Nelson, it was gradually eaten up
by Governments which wanted to reduce taxation in order
that they might whoop about it at next election, and
thereby secure votes.
* * * *
Premier Seddon estimates the Maoriland surplus
for next year at £31,835. As this isn’t nearly enough to
keep the Public Works Department going—it wants about
±600,000 a year—there must be more taxation or else a
loan. And as Seddon hasn’t the pluck to face the taxa-
tion there is going to be a loan. What is wanted is a
separate tax, estimated to produce £600,000 a year, which
shall be paid into a separate Public Works fund so that it
can’t be used for ordinary expenditure purposes. The
Works Department shouldn’t be dependent either on the
precarious surplus or on the miserable borrowing ex-
pedient.
• • * •
The A. J.S. Bank directors have wisely decided
to pay no dividend this half-year. The profits are ±20,629
(equal to a little over per cent, per annum) as com-
pared to the four previous half-years’ returns of £25,825,
±24,932, £21,914, and £32,083. During the last two years
the A. J.S. has reduced its reconstructed deposits by some-
where between £1,000,000 and £1,500,000 —the exact
amount can’t be ascertained because the sheet mixes up
the long-dated deposits due 1907 and the new deposits—
which means a large saving of interest; it has also effected
a reduction of interest on some of the converted deposits,
and in addition it has increased its capital by over ±120,000.
And, despite all this, the net earnings have declined over
±ll,OOO as compared with the corresponding half of ’94.
Whether the much-reduced profit of £20,629 has been
earned in the ordinary course of business or not is uncer-
tain, but one circumstance seems to suggest that it has
not. For some years past “ British Consols £240,600 ”
has been an item in the balance-sheet. It appeared there
when Consols stood at par, and, as the figures never
altered, it is evident the bank did not take credit for the
greatly increased value of the security. This half-year it
disappears, so evidently the securities are sold, and as the
increased value realised, which represents fully £21,000,
doesn’t appear anywhere else, Thk Bulletin assumes that
it is included in the profit. If this is so, it accounts for
all the profit, and the ordinary business would seem to
have yielded nothing at all. And, again, if this is so, the
outlook for next half-year, if things don’t improve mean-
while, is very unpromising.
The A.J.S. adds =B2OOO to reserve, making it
£52,000, and (including the balance from last half-year)
has ±21,264 to carry' forward. The reserve, so far as
appears, isn’t specifically invested in Government securi-
ties or otherwise set aside, but seems to be mixed up in
the general business. The cash (£804,662) is £230,000 less
than it was six months ago, and the total of cash and
liquid securities is £1,316,628, or £546,000 less. The re-
payment of £500,000 of fixed deposits accounts for most
of this, and the remainder of the decline is apparently
part of the general badness of things. During the
six months the reserve-fund of the old bank has declined
by £199,000, which apparently means that that amount
has gone to wipe off bad debts and depreciation in the
old assets. The old bank’s reserve has diminished in two
years from £738,164 to £374,212, so it has been vanishing
much faster last half than it did before. Whether the
remainder of the reserve is sufficient to cover all losses
and depreciation not yet provided for is a point which
remains to be settled. As compared with last half-year
the note circulation shows a decline of £25,000 ; current
accounts have dropped by £91,000 ; and taken all round
the A. J.S. looks less promising in almost every direction.
• * * *
The Queensland National Bank also stops pay-
ment of its dividend this half-year, and announces that it
will have to join in the reconstruction movement. It
wants a reduction of deposit-interest, and also more time
for repayment. The half-year’s profits are £10,021, against
£13,130 for the previous six months —equal to about 2J
per cent, per annum on capital. There is very little in-
formation in the Q.N.B.’s sheet. Deposits have appa-
rently declined £600,000 during the half-year ; but, as all
manner of deposits are lumped together, it is impossible
to say whether the reduction is caused by a great ske-
daddle among the currtnt-account holders, or among the
new fixed depositors, or through the partial repayment
of reconstructed deposits, or through acceptance of re-
constructed deposits in payment of debts, or through
reduction in Government deposits, or whether there have
been £1,000,000 or more of private deposits withdrawn
and the reduction has been partly counteracted by an
increase of Government deposits, or what has happened.
At all events, nothing has been heard about the Q.N.
Bank paying off any of its old deposits. The cash and
more or less liquid assets are set down at £2,132,043 —a
decline of £169,000 during the half-year ; and the fact
that, despite this apparently big reserve, nothing has
been said about releasing any part of the deposits looks
like a confession of weakness. The assets consist mostly
of “ bills receivable and all other debts due to the bank,
£7,763,781,” and there is nothing in this large, con-
gested item to throw any light on the real value of the
securities. In fact, the balance-sheet of the Q.N. Bank
contains as much obscurity as the worst of the banking
reports of Australia. The Q.N. Bank has no reserve-fund
now, its old reserve having vanished in ’93, and its capital
(£887,000) is about the weakest in Australia in proportion
to liabilities.
* * ♦ *
The Bank of North Queensland still goes on its
troubled way with moderate success. The half-year’s
profits are £1702, as compared with the last three half-
yearly records of £llB2, £1736, and £1674. There is
therefore a handsome improvement on the returns at 31st
December, ’95. Still the profit is less than 2 per cent, per
annum on capital. As usual, there is no dividend, but
the balance to the credit of profit and loss is now ±5648.
Dividends, with the 8.N.Q., seem still a very long way
off. Deposits (£231,619) show an increase of £40,000 dur-
ing the half-year. The coin-reserve is £57,709, and the
coin and liquid securities are ±87,747 —a large increase,
and equal to 31 per cent, on liabilities. The peculiar
strength of the B.N.Q. lies in the fact that, out of the
±390.000 Of advances, more than one-half is the share-
holders’ own capital, so that the assets could depreciate
aboqt 60 per cent, all round before there would be any
risk of loss to depositors or other outside creditors. In a
large bank it often happens that the shareholders’ capital
equals a much smaller proportion —in some cases it goes
as low as one-eighth—of the advances, and in these latter
instances a 20 per cent, depreciation in assets would eat
up the capital and make a very big hole in the deposits as
well.
About a year ago the B.N.Q. made a new
departure by taking 4£ per cent, deposits fixed for 10
years and repayable thereafter only at the option of the
bank. These now amount to £47,000. The idea evidently
was that though a bank could only, pay, say, 3 per cent,
on ordinary deposits, where a large portion of the money
had to be kept lying idle to meet repayments, it could
afford 4£ where there was no repayment save at the bank’s
convenience, and consequently all the money' could be
profitably invested. The scheme seems to have produced
no dreadful results so far, though 4| per cent, is a high
rate and 10 years a long term. A year ago the B.N.Q.
wrote off another £lO,OOO to cover depreciation, but
whether this finally wipes off all losses or not is unknown.
If the £389,598 of advances are all good, they only earn
£4 19s. percent, per annum gross interest, and there is
very little margin in taking deposits at £4 10s. and in-
vesting them at £4 19s. and paying working expenses out
of the difference. And if the average lending rate is, say,
6 per cent, it means that fully £60,000 of the investments
are yielding no return at all, and if even half of that
amount has gone bad it will take about nine years at the
present rate of profit-making to wipe off the shortage.
For these reasons the present writer would much rather
be a 41 per cent, depositor than a shareholder in the
B.N.Q.'
* * * *
Taking things all round, the first half of ’96 has
been a disastrous time for banks generally. The Commer-
cial of Australia earned 1 25,134 less than the previous
half-year; Bank of Victoria, 15563 less; City Bank of
Sydney, £l9ll less;, Ballarat Banking Co., £213 less;
A.J.S. Bank, £5196 less; Queensland National Bank,
£3109 less. Three small increases are the Royal of
Queensland, which earned £413 more, the Bank of
North Queensland £521 more, and the Commercial
of Sydney', £829 more than last half, but they
amount to very little amid the general decline. The
banks which issued their statements on 30th March
were also in the main an unfortunate-looking crowd.
The Colonial showed a serious slump—allowing for the
fact that it had got a large reduction of deposit interest ;
the National showed a decline of £3239; and the very
small improvements shown in the four boom-blasted pro-
vinces of the mainland were the Royal of Melbourne,
which was £6l better, and the Bank of Adelaide, which
improved by £177. In the face of all this general sinkage,
the stereotyped observation of the daily paper that the
balance-sheet was “most satisfactory” or “exhibited great
strength,” or “promised well for the future ” is a ghastly
mockery. The situation opens up a great field for con-
jecture as to the future. If any more big collapses took
place the result wouldn’t be a panic, for the country is too
tired and dispirited and soul-weary to rush for its money
as it did in ’93 ; but commercial Australia would simply
bur.v its head in the ashes, and turn its hinder part to the
cold blast of adversity', and want to give up the struggle.
* * * *
Queensland Mutual Insurance Co. shows a pre-
mium-income which is slightly', less than last y'ear’s, and a
profit for the year of £3351, against £4380 at June, 1895,
and £2901 in 1894. The usual 8 per cent, dividend ac-
counts for £1238, and the balance of £2113 is added to
profit-and-loss, making it £8246. Out of this, £4500 goes
to start a reserve-fund, which the Queensland Mutual
hasn’t yet possessed, the reserve to be invested in State
securities ; and £3746 is carried forward. The mam assets,
apart from £4950 of State securities, are baldly stated as
“ Investments, £9516,” and “ Mortgages, £3928.” Judging
by the interest received, which is nearly 4J4 P er cent, all
round, the securities seem to have escaped any serious de-
preciation, but “investments” doesn’t contain anything
like as much information as the shareholders should have.
Still, the Queensland Mutual seems a well-managed and
progressive office, and if it will keep clear of the big-
dividend craze until it has a reserve of £50,000 or so it
should have a good future before it. The yearning of
small new offices with hardly any reserves to pay 10 and
15 per cent, is one reason why the fire-insurance industry'
has run down so much in Australia.

Castlemaine Brewery Co. (Vic.) pays 6 per cent, for the

President Zeal made the funniest little speech
at the Commercial Bank demonstration last week. “ All
had been sufferers in the collapse, and must do what they
could to put the old bank right again, as well as to put a
brave face on matters and bring back prosperity to the
dear old colony.” Previously Zeal had said that “ thd
occasion was no time for carping, but for united effort to
pull the dear old country out of the rut (? rot) into which
it had fallen.” What a dear old gentleman !
* * * •
Matthew Davies’ brother, J.M., is one of the
sanguine solicitors to the Cotfimercial Bank. This is how
J.M. explains the old banking act which Attorney-General
Isaacs would amend —“ The Companies Act in England,
with reference to schemes of rearrangement, is defective
compared with the Victorian Act,” says Mr. Davies. “In
Victoria the scheme can be carried through the court
without winding up, but a scheme of this sort could not
he carried through unless the company concerned went
into liquidation.” Which means that according to Hing-
lish financial notions an institution that fails badly is
bankrupt, whereas in Victoria nothing ami nobody is a
bankrupt concern as long as the creditors will accept any
compromise, down to a halfpenny in the £.
Melb. business men thoroughly understand the
opposition of certain M’sP. to the Companies’ Bill. One
potentate’s connection with a somewhat smellful brewery
affair; another’s business relations with various banks,
and a host of similar matters, give the key to
the whole question. The public has invested its
money in the past in directors, and, having been bled, is
determined henceforth to have the Rontgen rays of in-
spection always in operation. The day of “ rallying
speeches” has gone for ever—such things would no more
allay suspicion than a mosquito-curtain would keep out
the cold. And well do the guinea-pigs of the past know it.
* » * *
At last meeting of Melbourne Met. Gas Co. the
wail of the high-price shareholder was heard in the hall.
He had paid a very heavy premium on his shares, and
his notion of fair dealing was that the gas-consumers
should be victimised for his benefit. All dissatisfied cus-
tomers had already left the co. and taken to kerosene,
and the directors would do well to pause before promising
to take off the extra tax they had lately clapped on to
those unfortunates who have borne with the extortion
thus far —said the wailer. The duty of the general public,
as far as this broad-minded capitalist can understand it,
is to furnish a profitable investment for any person who
walks blindfold into the market and buys gas-shares at
the current price. Robbery without Risk is Fat Man s
motto.
* * * *
Thornley, M.L.C., Vic., in supporting the pass-
ing* of the Seeds Adulteration and Sales Bill, the object of
which is to ensure to the buyer live seeds and seeds true
to name, instanced the case of a farmer in Western Vic-
toria who bought £lOO worth of alleged lucerne seed from
a dealer and only got three lucerne plants from it. The
Bill is bound to pass, as the seed swindle has been carried
for years beyond the limit of human endurance.

• * * *
The ex parte J. C. Pratt case, decided by N.S.W.
Banco Court a few days ago, besides making brewers and
hotel owners sit up, adds vast significance to the local-
option vote. It will now be necessary for every hotel-
owner, who does not wish to have the license cancelled
for at least three years, to personally see that the annual
fee is promptly paid. In the Pratt case, the tenant
applied for and got renewal, but failed to pay the fee
within the prescribed time, thus the renewal became void
and the house unlicensed. Then the owner brought an
ejectment action against the tenant, but did not recover
possession of the hotel for two months, and when he
applied for the license the bench refused it on the ground
that the 1.0. vote (taken meanwhile) was against further
licenses!
* * * *
The Junction has readied the lode (more com-
monly known as the western development) at 670 ft. deep.
At that depth, though the size of the lode is not yet
determined, the quality is quite satisfactory. At the
upper level where the body was first encountered the lode
was entered a dozen feet before the friable ore was got;
in this case that class of stuff was struck within 2ft. of
the wall. Up above the lode is about 80ft. wide ; down
here Manager Phillips expects it bigger—talks about
100 ft. But it will take time to prove, as the water is
making very heavy, and the pumps cannot cope with it,
so that work is hampered. Meantime, while the develop-
ment is entirely reassuring, it is as well to remember that
the market price of the shares has been steadily advan-
cing in anticipation of it.
The 670 ft. in this working of the Junction is
not, however, 670 ft. from the surface. The “ western
development” is in a flat, while, the measurements are
taken from the brace of a shaft on the outcrop. Neverthe-
less this is the greatest depth at which a solid, continuous
body of friable ore has been encountered at Broken Hub
In the South they have friable ore certainly at 800 ft. ; but
it is only in small patches.
I
Concerning the South, shareholders at Broken
Hill, who have been patiently watching the game for 18
months past, are apparently getting tired. At a meeting
held the other day they resolved to be directly repre-
sented at any forthcoming meetings “to protest against
the present methods of working the mine.” Perhaps no
mine has had more support from people on the spot than
the South, and none has given such tantalising results.
From Jan. 1 to June 30, the B.H. South treated
in its concentrating plant 29,951 tons crude ore, which
was reduced in bulk to 9424 tons, containing 6469 tons of
lead and 74,5230 z. silver. The saving was thus equal to
79-1 per cent, of the lead and 46;fi per cent, of the silver.
Or, up-ended, one-fifth of the lead and more than half of
the silver was lost. Yet, according to recent decisions of
the B.H. Proprietary directors, this jigging treatment is
the best yet evolved. Obviously “ the solution of the
sulphide problem ” is a haltinar thing up to date, espe-
cially as the South ore isn’t a bad ore to jig by any means.
Thus the week before last the 1321 tons crude ore treated
assayed in bulk 28 per cent, lead, s’7oz. silver, and 19’4
per cent, lead ; the product was 485 tons concentrates,
assaying 68"6 per cent, lead, 7"70z. silver, and 7"8 per
cent. zinc.
• * * *
A most peculiar mining accident occurred in the
Broken Hill Junction mine the other day. A miner at
the bottom of a shaft sent up a truck-load of rock. A
piece became dislodged, fell, cannoned against the side of
the shaft, and entered the arm just below the shoulder,
crashing through the bone and passing out. The wound
fwMoh was of a. most serious nature) presented all the
features of a bullet-wound.
* * * *
G-allymont Goldfields, Ltd.,” is the name of the
co formed in London to work a number of leases at
Galley Swamp, N.S.W., capital £300,000 ; purchase price,
£225,000, of which £150,000 is taken in fully-paid shares,
and £75,000 working capital. Sydney board of manage-
ment : Joseph Abbott, George Dibbs, and B. F.
Marks. It will only take 4000 oz. gold, free from all ex-
penses, to pay ashilling div., and, as the veins are small,
and the pay-chutes, so far, short, the manager has Ins
work cut out. The writer regards it as an appalling case
of over-capitalisation. The presence of such a local
board does not even excuse it.
* * * *
Paddy Lackey (N.S.W.) 500 tons for 4340 z.,
with 14 tons concentrates. Total value, £1734, which
should give a profit of £l2OO. This mine has been con-
sistently giving an oz. to the ton for years, and is owned
by four men. Locality, four miles from Sunny Corner.
Depth, 300 ft ; ore-chute, 450 ft., made up of lenticular
masses of stone averaging for the distance about 2ft. It
is under offer in England, and will probably be taken
over.
* * * *
New Royal Standard, N.S.W., 59 tons, 3190 z. ;
div (the 19th), Is. Length of ore-chute, 210 ft. ; size of
vein, under 6in., cut off to the north by a cross-course ;
unstoped ground, 80ft., and the vein good underfoot.
The co should now prepare to work the vein below the
tunnel-level, and try to pick it up the other side of the
fault. About six divs. of Is. each in sight.

Mt. Drysdale (N.S.W.) : Three weeks’ run for
86oz. gold; that should not mean more than 7dwt. per
ton And this is the mine whose 125 shares about 18
months ago reached £BOO each! It had all the advantages
for a sensation : it was splendidly isolated, worked a won-
derfully rich little pipe of ore—any statement that it was
a pocket was laughed to scorn-ore was shipped to
Wallaroo, and the mill was only put up when the rich ore
was exhausted. Everything sold and boomed around it,
and now everything is sickening. It is 30 miles from
Cobar but Cobarites got the best of the boom. Their
enei-n-ies are now devoted to the mines nearer home, and
they are chagrined and amazed that the phenomenal
richness of the Peak has not given them another boom.
Cobar forgets that in N.S.W. once bitten in a boom means
40 times shy of anything within 100 miles of the same
town. , * . *

* * * *
The Lady Brown (W.A.) syndicate has promptly
resolved itself into the Lady B. Gold Mining Co., no
liability—nominal capital £25,000 in 50,000 shares of 10s.
each. The 200 syndicate shares carry 200 shares apiece in
the co., and the balance of 10,000 shares is held over by
the directors for disposal at their discretion, whilst they
hunt for gold with the £lOOO balance of syndicate cash m
hand, and wait for something to turnup. Seemingly the
British capitalist is expected to turn up.
* * * *
Star of the West, Broad Arrow, 33 tons, 9240 z.
—a splendid return ; but the dead-and-alive character of
the small-crushing-big-return W.A. mines prompts the
question, “ How long will it last?” Where are the yields
of several others —for instance, Hill End, also at Broad
Arrow, and Hayes’ Hit-or-Miss in the White Feather dis-
trict. Both had their sensational small crushings, and
the owners of each sold out at big figures—Froggy getting
one, and Bull the other.

Kalgoorlie.—“ The Slug Hill Consols has struck
rich gold again. ” What does it mean ? Rich for a few
hours, and then poor for as many months ?
* * * *
Norseman—" An ironstone formation has been
found six miles S.E. of Norseman, called Mt. Jagged. The
lode is over 45ft. wide, and gold is showing everywhere.’
In a state of nature, hundreds of big Westralian forma-
tions are full of gold, and, if you take an indiscriminate
sample, not showing very much gold, it assays all right;
but strange it seldom comes out in the battery or gets
into the bank. This clearly shows it is refractory. It
also depreciates in travel. Did not 400 oz. turn largely
into iron recently? And is it not a well-known and scien-
tifically attested fact that iron itself can be turned into
copper? These little troubles call for immediate atten-
tion of men up in the business.
* * * *
One W.A. mine, about which nothing has been
heard in the East for a year, has been re-opened, and big
blocks of stone, estimated to contain 800 oz. gold, have
been raised. These are to be sent to England. Alas ! are
specimens like this mere samples, or are they the mine in
bulk? J. Bullion has suddenly been struck with this
question : Why aren’t such magnificent things worked by
the finders ? Bull wishes he had thought of this before.
* * * *
The secretary of the Croesus Extended mine
writes a Kalgoorlie paper in defence of the directors’ action
in selling at Is. a share and 20 per cent, of the new co.
in shares, less 5 per cent, commission. The directors had
to guarantee the wages, as there was no money available,
and there was nothing in the mine to justify asking, a
higher price. And this is the mine into which some mis-
guided fools bought up to 7s. a share on the advice given
and repeated 100 times by the great Sunday tipster!

Greenhills rush (Croydon way) has turned out
5000 oz. of gold since January, and nobody (according to
Hoolan, M.L. A.) has done a hard day’s work. The first
gold in red clay (north of Pt. Curtis) has been found on
this field, wherefore puddling machines are necessary.

kill the mine for a certainty, and break me up as a, mming
manager. —M.E. , * »

The marriage of Miss Buxton to Rev.
Bertram Hawker, at St. Peter’s Cathedral, Ade-
laide, July 23, was a gorgeous society affair. The
bride is much the handsomest of H.E.’s three
daughters, but she is extremely delicate. Mr.
Hawker, although S.A. born, is curate in charge
of a London slum-mission church, and the couple
return to London almost immediately. The
bridegroom has an income of about <£2ooo a year,
left him by the late George Hawker, the wealthy
squatter, once Speaker of S.A. Assembly.
* * * *
Capt. Guise, Governor Buxton’s serious-
minded aide, on the occasion of the recent vice-
regal trip to Oodnadatta, presented the aborigines
with a large parcel of tracts, which the poor nigs,
promptly presented to the local storekeeper,
thinking they were orders for groceries. Our
black brothers’ disgust on finding that the gallant
captain’s moral treatises on hell and damnation
weren’t convertible was unspeakable.

Anglican Bishop Harmer was to have offici-
ated at the Hawker-Buxton marriage in Adelaide,
but the Govt, doesn’t know for certain whether it
can legally recognise his signature of “J. R.
Adelaide,” and isn’t sure whether, in that elevated
capacity, he can perform marriages ; it has been
trying for many months to decide the point.
Many happy couples who were married by Dr.
Kennion are in fear and trembling.
* * * *
Although Adelaide Register devoted a
column to the description of the wedding-presents
to Miss Buxton, and two 1 and a-half columns to t
list of the dresses worn by invited guests, it care-
fully avoided mentioning the name of editor
Bonython, of the rival daily, who gave a costly
present, or of his wife and daughter, who attended
the reception.
* * * *
Symon, Q.C., gave the S.A. Governor's
daughter, as a wedding-present, a photograph of
Auldana vineyard, of which he is proprietor. It
is not stated whether or not it bore the legend :
“Buy our best claret.”
The Anglican Bishop of Newcastle, preach-
ing a special sermon in St. Andrew T ’s Cathedral in
Sydney, received only a couple of inches notice in
SiM. Herald, whereas Card. Moran, preaching
an ordinary sermon at Bondi on the same day, got
nearly a column of report. The Nonconformist
conscience, struggling with the appearances of
things, is wonderful in its strategies. In this case,
probably, the Herald was thus moved because on
the Monday before it had given several columns
to the celebration of a blaring Orangeism !

The appointment of Col. Spalding to be
Resident at Norfolk Island is a not wholly-unex-

A N.S.W. lady, in filing her schedule the
other day, described herself as “ a widow out of
business.” When you come to think of it, it really
is a business to be a widow.
* * * *
The defendant in a matrimonial court at
the inevitable North Melbourne, the other day,
declared that when he gave his wife money she
spent it on Chinese paint for her complexion
Complainant declared she spent what she got ori
the bare, necessaries of life. The Bench sym-
pathised with the woman who endeavoured to
maintain a good face under adverse circumstances.
The Japs have come to stay in N. Queens-
land. At Thursday Island the little men and
their consorts have for years run a “conehuala'
with great financial success. At Childers, in the
Isis Scrub, there is already established a thriving
Japanese bagnio. In Bundaberg there are several
Japanese “ women ” competing with mean whites
for the profits of Kanaka lust; and at Mackay
there are barbers and nurse-girls. Piebald Queens-
land will make a pretty jewel in the Federation
ring.

There are vacancies for two churchwardens
in a Northern Baptist church. One dearly beloved
brother has eloped with his neighbour’s wife, and
another has been arrested for alleged embezzle-
ment, something like <£3ooo. All in one week.

perance Union negotiated lately
for a substantial proprietary in-

Some men have no nerves.
The other night a man roused up
a Sydney suburban police station
and said he’d fallen and ‘ ‘ sprained’ -
his leg. Investigation showed
that the bone of his leg was
snapped clean in two just above
the ankle, his foot hanging on by
much-lacerated flesh alone. Doc-
tors were sent for and the leg am-
putated. The only anaesthetic
administered was a stiff nip of
brandy !
“Let me tell
you,” writes a
N.S.W. solicitor,
“ about the worst
thing that an at-
torney can do is to
obtain an acquittal
for his client—in
capital cases, at any
rate. It is the agi-
tation created by
the sentence which
brings the lawyer’s
name into promin-
ence. A quiet ac-
quittal is ruination
tc a young practi-
tioner, whereas a
gorgeous hanging is
a mighty advertise-
ment. If Meagher
hid prevented some
oi las more notorious
clients from being
‘ scragged,’ he might
still be as obscure a
wrangler as a certain young ‘ shark ’
who, through being indiscreet
enough to disappoint Nosey Bob
three times in six months, is still
chewing pens in his office. Moral
to young attorneys : If you would be great, let
’em swing.”

Botany Bay law in Sydney. Witness : “I
decline to answer the question, because I may in-
criminate myself. ” Crown Prosecutor :“ I pro-
mise you, on behalf of the Attorney-General,
no bill will be filed against you.” A more or less
useless forensic debate followed on the point
whether a witness can be compelled to answer
questions that tell against herself. His Honor :
“ After what the Crown Prosecutor has said, are
you still afraid of being prosecuted ! ” Witness :
“No.” His Honor : “ Then you must answer all
questions just as any other witness must.” . . .
Ahem ! There should be but a short step between
getting evidence out of a witness by ensuring his
or her personal safety, and getting evidence out of
a witness by offering a monetary reward for it.

* * * *
The Parkes (jST.S.W.) women-shooting-
and-suicide case was an affair of Blood and
Kisses. The man called the woman names over a
money dispute. “ Kiss me and make friends,”
was his next mood. She declined. He went for
his gun and returned. “Don’t be frightened,”
says he. “ I’m not,” says she. He knocked her
down, and then—bang, bang at her and her
daughter ! “ I won’t fire any more. Come and
kiss me ” —to the daughter. The latter kissed
him. “ I’ll go for a doctor,” says he. And he
did, and came back and ki: ied the mother. Then
he went to a cricket-match. When the police
wanted to see the kisser, he blew his own head off.
Love, beautiful Love!
Melbourne’s Coroner philosophises :
“ When you are as old as I am you will know that
witnesses do not speak the truth. Indeed, accord-
ing to my experience, they never lie at their best
until they are bound by oath to speak the truth. ’
Dr. Youl likewise says that all women are liars, and
more subtle liars than men. If so, it is because
women pretend virtue everywhere, and the aver-
age man only in the witness-box.
* * * ♦
An intelligent and zealous burglar robbed
a Bitzroy (Vic.) hotel the other night. He hid
himself in the house before locking-up time, and
at 2 a.m. began burgling. The proprietor awoke
and, disgusted at having his sleep disturbed, pre-
pared to attack and rout the marauder. But Mr.
Publichouse is a one-legged man, and Mr. Sikes
had carefully removed his timber-limb and thrown
it into the street. Which recalls the old story
about the giddy timber-toed, old-time Sydney
official, whose leg “gay” ladies used to keep m
pawn until he had communicated with his wife on
the delicate subject of funds.

Something like a tragedy the
other day at Bendigo (Vic.) when one Bill Smith
was fined .£1 for singing “ Rule Britannia ” in the
streets and thereby annoying a Chinese open-air
mission. What’s the good of a Briton asserting
nowadays that he and his countrymen “never,
never shall be slaves” if a Chinese mission can
give him into custody and have him fined 20/-

Someone is howling that out of the 7046
aborigines in N.S.W. “only 400 are receiving re-
ligious instruction.” The rest are sucking-in
spirituous consolation in the shape of rum. But
you might as well give silk waistcoats to ourang-
outangs as provide religious instruction for our
black brother.

She died of consumption in a happy state,

Detective Goulder, of the Sydney police,
received promotion lately, and was duly compli-
mented in the press thereon, because of certain
acts of bravery allegedly performed by himi m
capturing two or three evil-charactered law
breakers. The promotion may have been light
enough, and the press eulogies right enough, so far
as the facts were concerned, but both were prema-
ture, and, therefore, reprehensible, inasmuch as
publication was made of them before the malefac-
tors were sentenced or tried.
* * * *
High-wind story from Westralia. A resi-
dent, suffering from toothache, walked two miles
to his home one night in the face of a gal . •
As he neared his destination the humane which
had become cyclonic, nearly wrenched his head
off, and finally blew him into a fence. He felt a
sharp pain, and then the offending tooth, which
had been raging a few moments before, ceased to
ache. When he crawled home in a lull he found
that the molar was missing. The wind had turned
dentist and pulled it !

There are now among Southern public
servants some creepy persons, who belong to
secret societies and wear rubber soles on their
prison-made boots, as they sneak about the office.
* * *
One of the sights of Sydney is the
gorgeously-fitted steam-launch with its uniformed
crew and the big flag flying astern, which calls
at Darling-street, Balmain, o’ mornings to take
Minister Young, of the God-fearing Cabinet, to
his office. It must cost the country a good many
pounds per an. to hump Minister Young--did he
swear off the penny steamer when he got his port-
folio?-—to his office. If the other Ministers arrive
at their offices in the same large and dignified
manner, the bill should be something consider-
able.

* * ¥ *
An Orange curse lay thick upon the Melb.
papers on the day after the Brunswick shindy.
In addition to the columns about that event, and
the news of the foundering of the Orange Grove,
the Age published an article largely about
Mildura oranges. It said :
And although we do not nowadays hold an orange in
our hand while being photographed, as the family of the
good Vicar of Wakefield did on a famous occasion, our
affection for the golden apples of the Hesperides has by no
means diminished with the greater plenty and cheapness
of modern days.
Now, Goldsmith’s vicar and family (see chap. 16)
did not have their photographs taken at ( all,
because photography was not thought of in Gold-
smith’s time ; nor did they have their portraits
“ limned ” with oranges in their hands. It was a
neighboring family who did that, and the worthy
vicar thought the orange-pose “a thing out of
taste.”

It is not generally known
that when the large iron sup-
ports of the Murray bridge were
being cast, one of the workmen fell
into the molten mass. He is there
still. Not a vestige of him was found.
* * * *
The Adelaide doctors who recently assem-
bled to curse at large over the local hospital dis-
pute not only u resolved ” that the action of the
two physicians who have accepted permanent
billets at the hospital is “ highly dishonorable, ’
but “ expressed their strong disapproval of cer-
tain other doctors who have been carrying on the
hospital work since the old medical staff went on
strike. Whatever the merits of the dispute may
be, if these other doctors hadn’t stepped in to
temporarily fill the gap the patients would presum-
ably have been left to rot in their beds without
medical attendance, and the patients were in no
way responsible for the row. Therefore, the
individuals who “ strongly disapprove of this
act of humanity certainly look like a most inhuman
and bowelless crowd of bone-sawyers.

PARTED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When summer comes in her glory and brave the
whole earth blows;
When colors burn and perfumes impassion the
gladden’d air,
Then methinks thy laughter seeks me on every
breeze that goes,
And I feel thy breathing warm about me every-
where.
Or in the dreamy eve, when our soul is spread in
the skies,
When Life for an hour stands hush’d, and the
gaze is wide to behold
What day nor night may show, then sure it were
no surprise
To find thee beside me sitting, the piteous eyes
of old.
But’, alas ! when winter rains drive hard on the
blacken’d pane, _ _ .
And the wail of the lonely wind is lost m the
waste outside,
When the room is high and chill and I look for
thy face in vain,
I know that seas plash cold in the night and the
world is wide.
C.B.

been tested by the N.S.W. Fresh Food and Ice

Time deals gently with the woman who
uses “ Babeskin ” soap. It softly removes the
tell-tale lines of the years, and renews the delicate
bloom of youth. One shilling packages of two
cakes. Washington H. Soul & Co., Pitt-street

The Cameo is the boss cigarette of the uni-
verse. Everybody who has tried it says so, and
the person who hasn’t tried it is so inconsiderable
in point of numbers as not to be worth mentioning.
Likewise he is a sad, joyless person who has missed
what should have been the brightest episode of his
life. The Cameo cigarette is poetry, art, the music
of the spheres, the breath of the zephyr on the hills,
food, drink, raiment, literature arid placid, un-
worldly contentment all in one, and he who doesn’t
know it isn’t alive, yet isn’t definite enough to
know that he is dead.*

Sydney, August 5, 189(5.
My Dear Moorabinda—
The big Masonic charity ball in Sydney Town
Mall last W edresday was so crowded that only a
Kontgen ray could have got through the room five
minutes after starting-time. The public like a
massive Masonic ball, because there’s so much
circus in the ingredients, and because the catering
is always done on a colossal scale, as if the com-
mittee expected a school of sharks to supper.
Ihe last ball began as usual with a Noah’s Ark
procession of the worshipful brethren, who entered
under an arch of steel (rather suggestive of bul-
locks running the gauntlet of the slaughter-yard),
to mysterious monotonous music, each Mason with
ms glorified dilly-bag and tawdry horse-collar and
weird Masonic jewels, which I am assured are
costly and genuine, but which always look panto-
mime to me. After a walk round, which the pro-
cessionists took very gravely indeed, they halted
in a hollow square near the music, each worshipful
brother in his place with his toes turned out
When you think that the Masons only bubble up
m this magnificent style once per annum, it reflects
great credit on the man at the wheel that Brother
Blank s annual complicated manoeuvres don’t land
him in the middle of George-street instead of in
his apportioned position. But all goes well in-
variably— to the disappointment of the galleries
no doubt. ’
Of course, it all looks a lot of solemn ado about
nothing, and it’s a pity, from a picturesque point
ot view, that there is no standard height for a
Mason, for the profile of the procession, so to
speak, had a wavering irregularity that would give
a conscientious stage-manager the hump. Also
there is no stupid prejudice as to what color a
Mason ought to be. Three Japs (and that’s not
counting Quong Tart, who is vaguely understood
to be a Dutchman) turned up adorned with the
handkerchief sachet of Masonry, which is evi-
dently an evolution of the primeval fig-leaf ; and a
dusky y«uth, who might call Othello his uncle with
some show of apparent probability, also sported
the regalia. At midnight the ladies denuded the
e Masons of their vanities (women are ever on the
and sported the baubles themselves in the
me Lancers. Most of ’em hung the sachets
round their waists, which struck me as being an
error of judgment in some cases. They would
have been much more effective higher up.
_ Only a small spoonful of the salt of the- earth
flavored the Masonic ball. Gov. Hampden
turned up unadorned. His Ex.’s effective silvery
hair and dark eyebrows are quite wasted in
modern evening-dress. The Gov. as an “amber
snuff-box and a clouded cane ” (added to some
flowery tadormg, of course) would make a greater
effect than Kyrle Bellew in a powder part.
Lady Hampden (her Ex.’s dancing is really
of a most unstudied order) wore Wonga-
pigeon blue brocade with a cream brocade
petticoat and sleeves of electric-blue velvet.
Lhe Y.-shaped bodice was draped with snuff-
color lace and a sprinkle of diamonds; and a
com. bouquet, not forgetting her Ex.’s double eye-
glass, completed a rather pointless ensemble. I
often think it can’t be quite respectable to dress
well Australia the “ nicest ’’ people invariably
suggest that the tabs of their boots aren’t tucked
in. Miss Brand was effectively simple in blue
tulle and forget-me-nots ; her sister in cream bro-
cade with fakements of sapphire-blue velvet. But
a rococo dame of the V.R. party, in black with a
scarlet head-dress, reminded me of an enraged
rooster. The floor was good, the music of the
fashionable rapid tempo (Mrs. Ludlow played her
popular Crescent waltz for an extra and was
roundly applauded, and Miss Sylvia Blackstone
also played one of her sprightly waltzes), and the
decorations were impressively hung up.
Do you approve of the common vice-regal habit
of departing on top of the supper, as it were?
At big shivoos Her Majesty’s representatives
dangle about the dais until an excited committee-
man leads ’em off two by two to the region of
corkscrews, after which they take a hurried depar-
ture as if something they had eaten had not agreed
with them. For ordinary persons to rush for°their
wraps the moment after their hosts had furnished
forth sustenance would look rather greedy—as if
cold chicken was their magnet and not the per-
sonal charm of their entertainers—but then the
ordinary person has to be a lot more particular in
matters of this kind than Govmentouse. For
example, ordinary mortals generally learn to dance
before they plunge into a ball-room, whereas the
ordinary vice-regality seems to have only vague
rudimentary notions of the teetotum art, but
calmly faces the music nevertheless, though in the
very densest fog as to what will happen next. For
people who know positively that Fate will place
them at the top of the set, the disjointed manner
in which they amble about—in quadrilles espe-
cially—shows that preparations for an expected
contingency is not a V.R. virtue.
The show of “ diamonds ” on the floor at
the Masonic ball was simply paralysing. One
lady in ruby plush must have been worth half
Coolgardie, if all her stones were genuine ; and a
prominent bookie’s wife, in pink silk, sported a
really glorious diamond necklace —every stone in
it an eloquent sermon to punters.
Apropos jewellery—l believe I would rather see
counterfeit diamonds worn at night than real
diamonds on a woman in the morning. I lately
endured the agony of sitting opposite a Madame
Midas at breakfast, and she wore sealskin and
real diamonds. Ugh ! Diamonds for breakfast
is like the whiff of a stale cigar the day after you’ve
made a night of it. I know that be-diamonded
femininity era voyage urges as an excuse that jewels
are safest on the person—which well may be. But
there is no earthly reason why they shouldn’t wear
em under neath until the sun sets.
Sydney Amateur Opera Co. is going to break
out in (ye gods!) “Maritana” at “one of the
leading theatres,” which vagueness looks as if the
opera co. hadn’t yet discovered which was the
cheapest show-place. Why amateurs should want
to play “Maritana” I fail to see. One would
think the wig of the King of Spain and the lace
round his inexpressibles would be enough to scare
off any intelligent person—and the certainty that
the amateur Lazarillo will burst into tears as well
as tights on the eventful evening, and finally go
on draped in a tablecloth, might well give pause
to the most daring management. However
?° V “Faust,” “Kip Van Winkle,”
Club Fife, and “His Royal Highness” up its
sleeve. So the last state of the Amateur Opera Co
may be better than the first. On Tuesday, Aug.
11, the Charles Huenerbein-conducted concert m
aid of existing’ charitable institutions, and under
awe-inspiring patronage, including a party from
Oxuv’ment House, comes off at Sydney Town Hall
Mrs. 1. J. Riley, Maud “Heather Waltz” Fitz-
Stubbs, Miss Loveday, and Miss Rochester
(debutantes); a new German baritone, Herr
Staedtgen, Mons. Boffard, Poussard, Harry Les-
ton and others are in the swim, and tickets are 2s.
and Is., giving purchasers a chance to win a prize
m the art-union. Prizes include a hooded buggy
a bike, a Lipp piano, a sewing-machine, a bamboo
suite and a host of pictures. A. S. Gordon, Tem-
perance Hall, is the general secretary.
The latest courtesy captains extend to their pre-
sentable lady-passengers is doctoring their nails
for cm—finger-nails, of course, though I don’t
know what would happen if Trilby came aboard.
Poss'bly it s the reg’lation that captains should
attend to the claws of lady - passengers—l really
wouldn tbe at all surprised if it were. When you
come to think how women out-manoeuvre each
other to sit next to the boss of the boat, that
astute gentleman probably finds it a precautionary
measure to clip the dear creatures’ nails in the
event of the struggle becoming violent. And
there is no mistake how women burn to sit at Capt
Corcoran s right hand. When Arabella Goddard'
the pianist, was voyaging to Australia, she and an
Adelaide society-woman fought a pitched battle
over the question, and it’s on record that Arabella
won. Yet she played “Home, sweet Home”
divinely.

The cream of musical and long-haired Sydney
perpetrated the troublesome compliment of a sur-
prise-party to farewell the Anakian City Organist
Wiegand, whom they besieged at his private resi-
dence on Monday evening. The plurality of City
Organists’ farewells savors of the floats. To my
idea, the system of turning up in gala attire and
with smiling faces to signalise anybody’s going-
away seems scarcely the neatest way of turning
the compliment. But, after all, these things, like
morality, are matters of geography. There is a
dark a,nd dirty race in Africa who twine flowers
in their wool and do the high kick whenever a
corpse occurs—it is their way of showing their
bereaved feelings. Apropos musicians, a comical
feature of the furrin’ variety in Sydney is that no
matter what lingo they use in their calmer
moments, when they fall out they invariably
quarrel in English. But some of their insulting
epithets would hardly incense a Sunday-school.
“ Oh, y°u nice cup of tea ! ” and “ Go away, you
stoopid old sheep ! ” though not parlor language,
are scarcely calculated to provoke a serious breach
of the peace.
The Sydney Cercle Litteraire Francaise, so skil-
fully conducted by Madame Juliette Henri, had a
charitable fit last Monday, and broke out at Syd-
ney Gaiety in a Soiree Dramatique et Musicale
in aid of the Ashfield Infants’ Home. The infants
will be glad to know there was a large and swagger
audience. Madame Henri, in sumptuous array,
had the dramatic part of the biz. well in hand—
her own bright comedy, “Le Secret de la Mar-
quise, oil les deux Meres,” going very briskly.
Miss Pedley. and the St. Cecilia choir (I don’t
know why it is, but I always expect these warblers
to come on with their bare feet and their back-hair
down) womanaged the musical part, which lacked
snap—Mons. Boffard getting the only encore of
the evening for a sweet, somewhat feminine
rendering of “ David singing before Saul.” The
theatre was gorgeously dolled-up for the occasion.
Sydney fashions must be getting risky, to say

only those and nothing more scarcely

charming little girls or spirited baby son and heir,”
and so on—judging by the nauseating frequency
) vlfc “ which such marmaladey allusions are made

Roaring Gimlet, a Westralian field de-
rives its name from the extraordinary noise made
m a number of gimlet-trees (twisted bastard box)
which had been set fire to by the pioneers. The
sound was like the bass-notes of a mighty organ.
Pestle : “ Scotty ” overlooks one thing in
writing re grog-making. They now pump air
through the whisky to oxidise or volatilise the
aromatic ethers, &c., which are so abundant in
new whisky. Hence “ old matured whisky whilst
you wait. ” J

There now seems to be a hopeless breach
between the members of the (old) N.S.W. Art
Society and the (new) Society of Artists, and
further salt was rubbed into the wounds the other
night by the recountal at an Art Society meeting
of how somebody connected with the Artists’
Society had contemptuously referred to Art
Society members as “ tripers ” and u dingbats.”
Graham, M.P., Vic., declared the other
night that many of the Govt, stock inspectors
didn’t know a sheep from a goat, or a bullock
from a bunyip, and related how an inspector on
the Murray wanted to turn back a mob of
sheep because they were ear-marked ; he took the
ear-marks to be indications of some new disease.
The drovers at last convinced him that those were
a peculiar breed of jumbucks, born with holes in
their ears.
The Courier (Brisbane) reporters have
had their salaries raised to normal standard ; the
final instalment—the raising has been done by
degrees— was made a week or two ago. The
linotype came, the poor comps, went, and the re-
porters shared in the plunder. Apropos, when a
corpse was found in the river and the police re-
ported it by telephone to the Coukiek, with the
usual—“and nothing of value was found on the
corpse,” the city editor used to call the staff
together and count them to see who was missing.

White y-Brown.
Pursy Ch irnside has arisen in Vic. Assem-
bly to repudiate an alleged suggestion that he
offered a little bit of Werribee estate to the
Minister of Railways with a view to getting a
tramway run through his property to the adjoins
ing sewage farm. He had no such scheme in hi-
iriind, it appears. Personally he is a patriot,
“ without a thought for his own pocket or his own
interest.” Also, he “regards the word ‘Honor’
as above every other word in the English lan-
guage, and would sacrifice anything rather than
do a dishonorable act.” Tit,:: Bulletin accepts
Pursy’s explanation, and, in a metaphorical sense,
passes a gentle hand across his narrow young
brow, and congratulates him on being the first
person to emphasise the word Honor in the Vic-
torian Parliament since poor old Peter Lalor left
the chair.

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners win find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.
Premature baldness is becoming quite
characteristic of Australians. Entering the dress-
oircle of any theatre and gazing down on the stalls,
one is surprised at the formidable array of shiny,
hairless heads. The prevention of this disfigure-
ment seems to be in everyone’s own hands. Mr.
Edwin Holland, of Melbourne, whose regular an-
nouncements appear in The Bulletin, has made
a life-long study of the hair and its diseases, and,
judging by his testimonials, is pre-eminently suc-
cessful in his specialty.*

Tasmania and Queensland put the eligi-
bility of Judges to sit as members of Federation
Convention to a Parliamentary vote. Queensland
pronounced them eligible, Tasmania ineligible.
Speculator M‘Eacharn has his syndicate
now ready, and proposes to submit without delay
definite proposals to Brisbane Parliament, concern-
ing the taking over of the Queensland railways.
“No Irishman in our Ministry,” echoed a
N.S.W. Freetrader, the other day; “in the last
90 years only two Irishmen sat in a .British
Cabinet. ” But how does Reid apply that historical
fact ?
Much tuss is made in S.A. re the advance
of <£60,000 to village settlements, but little is
heard about the hundreds of thousands distributed
amongst the squatters for pastoral improvements.
During the next three years <£350,000 becomes
due from the State to squatters in respect to
leases then falling- in.
The railway across S.A. to Port Darwin,
mooted by a foreign eo. (divs. to be State-
guaranteed, of course), was a pet scheme of con-
tractor Brassey (progenitor of the Vic. Gov.) who,
in the seventies, negotiated for its construction by
his firm on the “grid-iron” land-grant system.
The deal just missed fire.
In obtaining from N.S.W. Assembly a
three-months’ Supply Bill of over £2,000,000
Premier Reid has cajoled, by the bribery of muni-
cipalities and of “labor,’’from a. recreant repre-
sentative House its first prerogative—the control
of the public purse. And, in politics, the handling
of the money is omnipotence.
SMITH AGAIN.
MRS. BROWN: “ Well , well —NOW I know at last where
all the Smiths come from!"
It doesn’t seem two years ago since Premie-
Reidwas asking the N.S.W. Freetrade-party to
join with him in adulating our staunch little
friend, M'Millan.”
NSW. Premier Reid, so far, flatly re-
fuses’to state how much the unpaid liabilities at
the end of the financial year amounted to. In
times past these have run as high as £1,000,000,
and as this is the first time they haven t been pro-
vided for in the year’s revenue, the public naturally
wants to know about them. The surplus of
£330,000 is no good if the liabilities that exist
against it run to twice or three or four times that
amount, and the fact that no information can be
obtained concerning these liabilities is ominous.
Queensland Leg. Assembly has
roused the wrath of the Northern
Civil Service by raising members
salaries from £l5O to .£3OO per annum.
The Q. Civil Service grievance hes in
the fact that Northern public officials
were severely retrenched three years
ago far beyond the intent of Parlia-
ment, and it is asserted that justice
should have been done in this matter
first of all.
N. S. W. Postmaster-Gen. Cook:
“In Victoria, where there was no free
postage, 15,000,000 papers a year
went through the post. In N.S.W.,
where postage was free, the number
wa535,000,000.” Why, cert’nly. And
if N.S.W. went a step further, and
gave everybody Id. for sending a
paper through the post, the number
would probably be 6,000,000,000, or
something like that.
N.S.W. Leg. Council's agree-
ment to reduce the cost of miners
rights from 10s. to ss. per annum is
another step towards making the
avenues to work easier for the feet
of the worker. The first diggers’
right issued in Australia was charged
for at the rate of 30s. per month.
Later the price went up to 40s. a,
month and then suddenly slumped
to 40s. per quarter, and for the last
15 or 20 years has generally run from
10s. to 10s. 6d. per year. The next
move will probably be. to a figure
which merely corresponds with the cost of the
instrument to the State.
The Maoriland Opposition attempted a want-
of-confidence vote against the Seddon Government
over its borrowing proposals. Seeing that a very
large part of the proposed <£1,000,000 is for unpro-
ductive jobs which will return no revenue, there is
good cause for complaint, but the spectacle of the
great borrowing party asking the people to put it
in office on such a plea is a gorgeous piece of
assurance.
N.S.W. Premier Martin put postage on
newspapers in 1864, Parkes took it off in 1874, and
Premier Reid, through his automaton, Cook, in-
dicates that he will leave things as they are.
Free-postage of newspapers can only be upheld as
a straight-out bribe to the metropolitan daily
press. “Itis a concession to Education yet, it
you put Shakespeare or the Word of God into the
post you have to buy stamps.
N.S.W. “truck” legislators
should stick closely to the spirit and
letter of the following clauses from the
English Truck Act: “No workman to
be liable in debt for goods bought at
any shop in which his employer is
interested ”; and “No interest, to be
charged on advances of wages. In
many of the most reputable workshops
both these clauses are required in their

A Maoriland law recently passed
enacts that the domestic Biddy shall
have a half-holiday every week, ihe
Tory papers allege that, aooordmg to
good legal opinion, the Act is so rigid
that the Biddy must be ejected from
the house not later than 3 p.m. on her
half-holiday, and not admitted again
till 10 p.m., because, if she is found
on the premises between these hours,
she is presumed to be kept at work,
and her mistress is therefore liable to
be fined. And it wants to know what
will happen if it is snowing heavily,
and the slavey is ill in bed, and is so
heavy that her mistress can’t carry her
downstairs and throw her out into the
weather for the specified seven hours.
Which looks like a fine, humorous lie
_ofthe kind that the Maoriland Ass.
press revels in.
“New Guinea,” says Bishop
Stanton, “ has been handed over to the
missionaries.” By what right ? MTI-
- tried to steal New humea, but
was discouraged by the British Govt.,
which afterwards, “on its own,” stole
only a part of it. The missionaries
are now, if the Bishop’s words were
judiciously chosen, the receivers of
stolen goods. Australia maintains the
cost of the robbery. Why, therefore,
should the missionaries alone enjoy the
loot? Buccaneers all, strike for your
rights !
N.S.W. policy— Government by Contra-
diction, Logrolling by Select Committees and
Administration by Boards. And it takes 120 paid
members of the elected House to see it all done.
There is a small dissension in the S. A.
Labor-party’s camp. Wood, the Labor candidate
who beat Harrold for the Tory district of N.
Adelaide, stated in the House that he would vote
to turn out the Kingston Ministry—-m a word.
Wood has estranged himself from his party, and
special meetings have been held to consider what
steps shall be taken. Wood declines to sign a
pledge to vote with the other 17 pledged Labor
men.

The anniversary disease is so virulent
just now that at the Sydney Home Rule
meeting the other night someone, remembering
that 1898 wall be the centenary of the Irish
rebellion, thought something might be done to
commemorate the occasion.
Yictoria, since it got a constitution of its
own has had 20 different Governors, acting and
actual. The longest term of office was that
of the first vice-rex, La Trobe, which Tasted
15 years. During the same period of the
province’s history it has had 16 Parliaments and
27 different Ministries. The shortest Parliament
was the tenth, which began 11th May, 1880 and
closed 29th June, 1880. The shortest-lived Minis-
try was the sth, Nicholson's, which only lasted four
weeks, and the longest the Coalition of Service,
Berry, Gillies, and Deakin, which practically ex-
tended over seven years.
The lesson of the budget-debate inN.S.W.
Assembly : A deficiency is a surplus, and a surplus
is a deficiency, except when either is a cross-entry,
and then both mean each and each means both ;
also, since everything in political finance is a cross-
entry, there is never a surplus that isn t a defi-
ciency, nor a deficiency that isn’t a surplus, nor a
surplus deficiency that isn’t a deficient surplus;
and if you multiply the debit surplus by the credit
deficiency, and divide the credit surplus by the
debit deficiency, and subtract each from eaoh, the
remainders are equal, and, the equal parts being
equivalent to each other, the corresponding errors
are equal eaoh to each, and—the Auditor-General
will please affix his signature right here. y. Jt.JJ.

The gorgeous new Houses of Parliament
which Premier Reid threatened to inflict on N.S.
Wales taxpayers are surely destined to go the way
of the castles in Spain. Does the Premier think
Federation is coming ? If he doe 3, what are these
gorgeous houses for? The Federal Parliament.
Then he’s counting his chicks before they re
hatched. If he wants to keep the other provinces
off, let him just say that and there’s an end to
Federation while he’s at the helm. Then, if the
Federal Parliament is elsewhere, what does
N.S.W. want with gorgeous palaces to house a
group of provincial politicians ?
“The present immense majority for Sed-
don,” remarks a Bulletin correspondent, “ is due
to having single electorates. The last election
was in ’93, and the votes polled for Govt., Opposi-
tion, and Independent candidates were in the
proportion of 4,2, and 1. Hence, to represent
public feeling, we ought, in a House of 70 mem-
bers, to have had 40 Seddonites, 20 Opposition,
10 Independent. And as, in many cases, there
was no Opposition candidates and no votes re-
corded, I think a fairer House would have been m
the proportion, 36, 23, 11. But the House, as
actually returned, was in the proportion, 51, 10, 4.
“Most millionaires are indifferent to
money. They simply love the game.”
When the Seddon Government established
co-operative works in Maoriland, it declared
in all simplicity of heart that married men should
have the preference over the uncoupled. As a
consequence, many of the works have become
dumping-grounds for female refuse. It has been
said that a demand for a tailor is not a demand for
a coat. In like manner, a demand for a married
man is not a demand for his wife.

Many entreaties were applied to Crrancl-
master Speaker Abbott to induce him to obtam
SHE THOUGHT SHE’D GET DOWN
HE : If we weren't on the bike I’d kiss you.”
SHE: “ I insist on dismounting instantly. ”
leave of absence from Parliamentary duty to open
the Masonic ceremonies in Sydney Town Hall last
week. Once before he preferred a social function
to his Chair-duties, and those who thought his
portly presence would enhance the picture of
mystic posing urged him to repeat the frailty.
But Speaker Abbott now knows that no social
function is permitted by the Constitution to inter-
fere with his Parliamentary duties. In the British
Commons that point was settled years ago. There
is record of a Speaker, over 150 years ago, who
accepted an invitation from the King of the day,
and was thereby late for the Chair, and, notwith-
standing that he pleaded the Royal command, he
had to submit to a formal censure. “ The Grand
Council of the kingdom is over all,” ran the
motion.

THE NEW ELECTORAL BILL\
The provisions as to aDsent electors give the
right to Vote by post. It an elector has reason to
I believe that he will be absent from his district on
[polling (lay, he can, after'the issue of the writ,
[obtain a certificate from the returning-officer, de-
Iclaring that he is entitled to vote for that par-
| titular district. The certificate is to be made
[on the back of an envelope, and Is to be forwarded
[ to the elector with a voting paper, containing In-
i' structions as to the method of voting. The elec-
tor, in his turn, must indicate the candidate for
whom toe intends to cast his suffrage by writing
the name of the candidate on the voting paper.
I Then he must, ih the presence of the nearest post-
-1 master, sign his name on the counterfoil of the
vfttlng paper. The elector must produce to the
'postmaster his elector's right, so that the signa-
| ture thefe may be compared with that on the coun-
I teffoll. “Then and there. In the presence only of
[ the postmaster,” the elector, must insert the vot--'
I ing paper in an envelope provided for the pur-
pose, and, “having seized the said envelope, must’
at once hand the same to the postmaster for de-
livery.” The returning officer who has isshed the
certificate must, meantime, have made a note of
| the fact, so that when the voting paper arrives,
: the signature may tye tested. Votes reaching any t
j returning-officer through the post must be -opened
| in the presence of the scrutineers; the counterfoil
bearing the signature must be torn oft', and the
! voting paper—which is supposed to be folded —
dropped in the ballot-box.

lucre ! A modern version of money-changers in

An old-time Vic. poet, whose “Poems”
are on the shelves of Melb. Pub. Library, is a
money-lender, and does good business in a quiet
way. Imagine a poet, however small, having money
to lend !
Colonel North, who died worth only a
paltry half-million in England, was a capitalist
elsewhere, it is now stated. The bulk of his money
went into real estate at Ostend and more distant
places.
The group photos, of the Johannesburg
Reform Committee in London illustrated papers
accentuate one noteworthy point. Out of the
whole 50 or 60 members, mostly rich men, not one
wears a belltopper.
Gavan Duffy always professed to believe
that his public services more than balanced his
pension. Barrister Ireland had the same plunder,
and Duffy was represented as asking: “Why is
Ireland poor and discontented ? I’m not.”
Charles Cowper is not the only N.S.W.
sheriff to whom the field of politics looked green.
William Adolphus Young, who was sheriff from
1842 to 1849, became member for Port Phillip in
the old N.S.W. Parliament, and later on obtained
a seat in the British Commons.
Did Shiels really commit the following
mixed metaphor attributed to him by the Argus :
“ There are two ways of letting daylight in. . .
There is the honest way of proclaiming publicly
that which should be known, and the cowardly way
of striking a blow in the face with the assassin s
dagger.”
When Parkes published his
“ Beauteous Terorrist ” (verses eulo-
gising Sophia Perovsky, who helped
her lover, Roussakoff, to dynamite
Czar Alexander II.), he was taken to
task by one of his-friends for praising
a murderess. Whereupon the old
man explained that he had very
strong feelings against the autocratic
power of the deceased monarch, who
had done very serious injury to the
Australian people. “ I confess I
can’t see what you mean, Sir
Henry,” said his friend. “ Well,
I’ll explain,” replied the veteran.
“You see, one of the most persist-
ent hactions of this hautocrat is to
force the ’Ebrews to leave Russia,
and the result is that many of these
people come to this country, where
they usually set up business as
money-lenders, and, being persons
without any noble hattributes, they
charge the most h extortionate hin-
terest, and hinsist on ’aving it paid.
That, Mr. ,is the reason why
I have tried: to himmortalise one
who hassisted to rid the world of a
hautocrat whose baneful hactions ’ave
made themselves felt in Haustralian
business matters.”
A Sydney publican, vvho began
his “ colonial ” career in Canada, was
once man-of-all-work to John Mao-
donald, when Premier, at the
latter’s Ottawa home. Old To-
Morrow had been away electioneer-
ing, and being expected home one
night, Lady M. thought she would
prepare for him the fatted fowl. Ac-
cordingly the “ man ” was ordered to
go to tne poultry-yard and kill two
of the finest birds he could secure.
Now, the Premier’s latest fad was
prize-fowl breeding, and he had just
before imported some splendid “ Ply-
mouth Rocks ” at great expense.
Fresh from Ould Oireland, the
“ man’s ” knowledge of choice poultry
was as slight as a politician’s virtue,
and, to the horror of “ my lady,” he
returned with two dead ten-guinea
hens as the result of his foray. Her
ladyship’s vocabulary (continually
being enlarged by new epithets in-
vented by her objurgatory husband) was not un-
equal to the occasion ; and the bird-slaughterer,
reckoning that he could not stand a second ora-
torical attack from the Premier himself, instantly
created a vacancy in his billet.
The Melbourne papers disagree about the
Prince of Wales. One describes him as being now
qtiite slim and the proud possessor of a waist,
whilst another declares that he has abandoned his
intention of riding a bike because his embonpoint
will run all over the machine. Tubby or not
tubby ? that is the question.
Marcus Clarke’s “ Long Odds, ” re-named
“ Heavy Odds,” is just re-published. The bulk
of it was penned by Walstab, through Clarke
getting his skull fractured out hunting, not long
after the novel begun in the Colonial Monthly.
Walstab’s “ Harcourt Darrell,” written when he
edited the Calcutta Englishman, was a brilliant
novel, drawing a warm eulogy from the Saturday
Review.
Jawbone Neild, M.P. (N.S.W.), is going
to Hingland. Part of his impulse is to drift
around and ask questions about “State Old-age-
Pensions ; ’ but the mainspring motor that starts
him off is a desire to emulate “ Tiger ” Inglis, ex-
M.P., in his literary achievements. Tiger only
got into comic prose and dialect humor. J. Bone
Neild soars into the blank blankness of the white-
hottest empyrean, to say nothing of the purply-
blue welkin of doggerel.

Edward John Eyre,
one of the earliest of Aus-
tralian explorers, who still
lives in England, will be
81 on Aug. 15. He emi
grated to Sydney in 1833,
and in ’36 conducted an
exploration party from
Sydney to Swan River.
In 1840, for the S.A. Go-
vernment, he went into
the interior, explored
Lake Torrens, traversed
the Flinders Range, and
thence travelled to King
George’s Sound on foot, his only English com-
panion (Baxter) and two or three natives being
murdered on the way. In 1847 he was appointed
Lieut.-Gov. of Maoriland under Hori Grey.

solicitor of seven years’ standing is eligible for
appointment to N.S.W. Supreme Court Bench,
he is not eligible under the Public Service Board
regulations for appointment as a police magistrate
unless he passes a fresh examination.

Ex-attorney Brady has been permitted to
employ himself in a “subordinate capacity” in
the Sydney offices of a highly respectable legal
firm. Those who know say that the Supreme
Court order that no disrolled solicitor should
engage in legal work of any sort without express
leave from the Judges, was framed in advance to
meet one case,, in which the man to be expelled
might have been appointed manager for his late
partner. But the little thing did not come off.

Premier Turner rejoices over one citizen
tax-payer who pays ,£2860 to the State as his
yearly contribution from income derived from pro-
perty. Another pays £llO9 from earnings derived
from personal exertions. The latter (if not Sur-
geon Fitzgerald) is probably a lawyer, although in
providing a lawyer’s income it is usually other
people who have to make the personal exertions.
Kingston and Symon bludgeon each other,
but there is no art in their invective. Neither
rises much superior to the infuriated fish-fag.
Pity that they should thus waste their golden
opportunities, for seldom have such noble chances
of enriching the literature of abuse fallen to two
public men. _ In nothing does humanity so dis-
tinctly show its improvement on the anthropoid
ape, with his coarse, scurrile jabbering, as in its ap-
preciation of the artistic niceties of educated
opprobrium.

the more insignificant members of the God-fearing
N.S.W. Ministry such as Cook, Garrard, and
small fry of that sort, is also tackling Premier
Reid. He complains that he can only get a sight

steady,” complains Simonetti, in an agonised tone

But why doesn’t Simonetti sculp the sandwich as
well as de mowf ?
Queensland papers seek to glorify the
°pgj n °f the late “Dingo” Donaldson, speaking
of his father as “ well-known in pastoral circles,”
aud so on. Donaldson used manfully to say his
L.ther had been a little farmer, and that he him-
self had carted firewood for a living as a boy.
“ Many a time I’ve gone home crying with hunger
and the load still on the dray unsold,” he would
say. “Dingo ’ was a strict teetotaller himself,
but would buy a dead beat a reviver if his tongue
looked dusty. But if Donaldson didn’t drink he
smoked. How he did smoke !
Old James Lynch, a patriarch who pegged
out recently at Clermont (Q.), was a man with an
experience. Fifteen years ago Lynch, who was a
good deal of a patriarch even then, was dying of
asthma in the local hospital, and his grave was
dug and his coffin made in readiness for his
decease. He would probably have died in due
course, only some inconsiderate person mentioned
to him that bis sepulchre was quite ready, where-
upon the infuriated old man arose from his bed
a Sr • s l olemn 1 1 y cursed the previousness of the
officials, and walked home. There was a coolness
between him and the hospital ever afterwards.
Prof- Haslam, of Canterbury College
(M.L.)j had a unique experience a few years ago.
He went to England, and, when about to return
cabled his wife— “ Coming home.” The cable
man knew better than he did, and rendered it—
Come home.” Mrs. Haslam accordingly packed
up and departed per next boat. When the Prof,
arrived he found his penates flown, and went off
again to bring them back, but his dutiful spouse
having arrived m England and found her lord
on the way to the other side of the globe again,
took passage in the next ocean steamer. Thus
they chased each other round the world for some
months.
Melb. commercial recollections are evoked
by the demise of Alfred Ross, a typical merchant
of the era so graphically depicted in Train’s
n J°?X?f mer u a Ab ™ a £” The members of

were Melbourne’s crack importers. Their
income at the zenith was .£lOO,OOO a year
and they had as many as 15 ships in Hob-
son s Bay at once. Spowers, the Argus man
came into the firm later. When Melb. Chamber
of Commerce started, 40 years ago, the West-
garths Rosses, Nanki veils, Raes, Lorimers, Moores
Dalgetys, and so forth ruled the community 3
the Argus at their core ; but there were two
traitors m the ranks, M'Culloch and Francis me
pared to lead the cause post-haste to destruction.
Old Lancashire cotton-spinners still rever-
ence the memory of a mill-owner named Richard
Seddon, who, during the horrors of the cotton-
famine, ruined himself by sticking to his hands
through thick and thin, and feeding all whom he
could reach and not on the usual rancid bacon'
and mildewed bread. A son of his of the same
Christian name went to Maoriland. Is Premier
Dick the man ?
A pleasanter existence—frequent change
oi scene and climate, with good company—than
that led by the dealer in brains whose portrait
appears in this column, cannot be imagined j
and he has led it for thirty years. It was iii the
early sixties that R. S. Smythe, then on the Par-
liamentary staff of an Adelaide daily, grew weary
of recording the utterances of colonial legisla-
tors and resolved to see the world. Having thea-
trical as well as Parliamentary duties, he knew the
show-folk of the period, and his original venture
was a couple of Italian opera-singers—the Bianchis
a fat tenor yoked with a still greasier prima-
donna ; and the first mystery into which the
ingenuous young agent was initiated was that he
must always engage separate apartments for the
harmonious yet amorous pair, or the tenor next
night would sing flat. Another of Sinythe’s early
stars was Margaret Aitken, a celebrated Scotch
elocutionist, who sold all the front seats for
her opening entertainment in the old Melbourne
Exhibition Building by announcing that the
committee of the Caledonian Society would sit on
the platform, which was a rather high one, in full
Highland costume, and who afterwards in Hobart
was cried by the bellman, a poor old exile whose
education was limited, as “the celebrated exe-
cutioner.” It was immediately after his engage-
ment with Miss Aitken that the man who was to
become known as the Much-Travelled (a title
originally conferred by The Bulletin) began his
world-wanderings, opening up the Far East,
India, and South Africa to European artists, so
that by 1871 he had met with so many adventures
by flood and field that his experience formed tha
subject of cm article in the Corn hill Magazine.
Yet it was after this that Smythe did most
of his globe-trotting—ostensibly in search of
talent, but often doubtless to gratify the lust of
travel—visiting most of the European capitals,
crossing America from Vancouver to Nova Scotia,
and touring Britain from Inverness to the Lizard.
Twenty years ago the Moltke of managers, as
Archibald Forbes called him, struck out an entirely
new path. Abandoning music and the drama,
and knowing the propensity of humanity for
gazing at remarkable men, even at murderers on
the gallows, he started that Jong procession of
celebrities, beginning with Proctor, the astronomer,
and ending with Mark Twain, who have provided
pleasant weekday evenings for thousands all over
Australia and Maoriland In this work R.S.S.
has been wonderfully successful, and there is no
doubt that his name at the foot of an advt. means
a good deal. Not that all his stars are of the
same magnitude. He will not run a nobody. A
man must have either a name or talent; yet it
would seem as if some of the inferior lights run but
not chosen by him are exploited at. their own r
If the public verdict is adverse, the engagenu t
R. S. SMYTHE , “ The Much-Travelled.”
Photo., Chuck, Ballarat.
is not renewed : they have had their day and cease
to be. In the musical world, to which he some-
times returns, as he did with Santley and the
Halles, the enterprising impresario only arranges
with the best; he abhors mediocrity, of which there
has been more than enough lately; he never touches
either fossils or infant prodigies; and it is
a striking instance of his critical judgement that
his contract with Paderewski was dated away back
in 1891, before the famous pianist achieved those
triumphs in Europe and America which have
hitherto prevented him from fulfilling his Austra-
lian engagement. Recently the veteran manager
has had an able lieutenant in his son, Carlyle
Smythe, at present travelling in South Africa
with Mark Twain, while he himself is arranging
the farewell seasons in Sydney, Melbourne and
Adelaide of the Rev. Charles Clark, the first and
best of the lecturers, upon whose like we are not
likely to look again.

LAND AGENT,
HAVING secured large areas of virgin soil
situate in the CAIRNS DISTRICT, N.Q.,
specially adapted for growing Sugar, Coffee, and
other tropical products, and convenient to Mills
in course of erection under the Sugar Guarantee
Act, is prepared to treat with intending Investors
on Easy 1 erms.
The lands offered compare advantageously with
those of Bundaberg or Mackay for important
reasons. Frost is unknown, damage from grub
nil, and the climate unsurpassed for the purposes
of such culture. To persons of small means this
opportunity should not be lost, as this industry
has been proved to be the best investment at pre-
sent offering in the colony.
Areas subdivided to suit purchasers. Hundreds
of Acres for Lease or Sale.
