SPORTING NOTIONS.
Minyarrah from London to The Bulletin :
“At Lord’s. England v. Australia. The thousands
assembled under the low forward-sloping, canvas-covered
frames, resemble so many myriads of black and white
ants peeping out from under inverted spade-blades. Later
the swelling band of onlookers surges out on the playing
area, fully 50ft. in some places, and makes the English-
men’s fourers easier and Australian deep fielding and
catching impossible. The ground will hold under 20,000
comfortably, and the M.C.C. comes in for strong adverse
criticism for allowing 10,000 more to enter than can be
accommodated. Those who cannot see amuse themselves
by throwing bombs of pebbles and paper at those who
can, and the police are powerless.
“ ‘ This team should be called the Trott-ers,’
said one disgusted Australian as his countryman proces-
sionized to and from the wickets on the Monday. That
first innings was one of the saddest, sorriest, and
sickest spectacle ever witnessed on any cricket-ground.
The ‘lB’ against ‘ M.C.C. and Ground ’ was almost- credit-
able in comparison to this total of ‘53 ’ on a first-class
wicket. 1 Funk,’ or nervousness, or that midway state,
equally disastrous, so hard to define? Richardson and
Lohmann, with the rattle of falling wickets around, and
flying bails before them, bowled splendidly. Donnan
idiotically ran himself out; Darling, Gregory, and Eady
batted with pluck, without luck ; the remainder —well,
they are Australians and there was a second innings.
“Richardson clipped Darling’s bails with a
‘ ringer,’ Eady found himself given ‘ caught behind,’ and
placed his hand on his breast—‘ more in sorrow than in
anger’—to show where the ball had touched, and the
second innings opened with 2 for 3. In the ‘ pros.’
pavilion, Albert Trott’s face, stretched by his dropping
jaw, obscured his shirt front, but visibly contracted as
‘ Joe ’ went to the wickets. What followed is ancient but
long-living cricket history.
“ Grudgingly it must be admitted that no Aus-
tralian audience could be more encouragingly enthusiastic
than was that Lord’s assemblage as Trott and Gregory
made their brilliant stand. At each progressive point in
the score the applause rose and spread in a thunderous
canopy, accompanied by loud and genuine cries of
‘Bravo,’ ‘Bravo’—the ‘ haw-haw’-ism of which mav be
excused for their heartiness. No more sportsmanlike
spectators were ever assembled round a ‘ pitch.’ They
wanted ‘Gallery’ Grace and his men to win, but never-
theless saw with impartial eyes and appreciated with
ready hands.
“ The Australians’ batting is much below par,
the bowling beyond expectations, but the fielding, in which
they were thought perfect, is by turns smart and faulty.
Had several missed chances been taken in this match
‘ the result might have been very different.’ Kelly will
never be a Blackham, or a Jarvis even, and with Hill,
Graham and Trumble drops catches a pennant player
would grip and hold like a mud-dredger. Jones fields
like the iron man he is, Eady follows him in patches,
whilst Trott, Gregory and Darling miss little.
At a big match F. S. Jackson is the prize pug-
pup on show with pale-blue ribbon knotted round its
neck and tail. England’s most dashing bat, the son of
the late Chief Secretary for Ireland, the Cambridge man
is a more obtrusively conceited cricketer than even being
one of the three ‘gentlemen’ to represent ‘England’
should encourage him to be. That such a man, wearing
pale-blue tinted pads and an all-pervading air of self-
suppurating conceit, can make runs is a downright dis-
couragement to the majority of modest ‘ cracks.’
* * * *
“If the real cause of Australian defeat in the
first big match was thoroughly sought,’ writes an Aus-
tralian abroad to The Bulletin, it would probably be
found that they were too well fed and housed. Tables
d’hdte, arm-chairs, and obese cigars are only suited to the
Fat Man waiting for dividends.
“ The defeat of the Australians by the Midland
counties should never have happened, and would not if
the ‘ Colonials ’ had put any real life into their work in
the early part of the game. It was only when defeat
loomed up that they seemed to awake, but then it was
too late.
“ Clem. Hill’s fine innings of 98 against the
Midland counties was only marred by the fact'that it was
two short of a hundred. He did not give a chance of any
kind, and played all the bowling with easy confidence.
He has had a bit of bad luck lately, but is certain to make
a lot of runs before the tour is over, and will probably
fight little Syd. Gregory for top place.
English people are most keen about winning
5? e r,^ st ' matches ’ and the aristocratic members of the
M.C.C. are most prejudiced. One gentleman (a colonel
by the way) got furious over the brilliant stand made by
Gregory and Trott, and kept ejaculating ‘ Curse them,
why the devil don t they get out ’ ; or if a ball bumped
high and was unsafe to touch, ‘ D you, tip it, touch
it and get caught, for God’s sake’ ! As the score mounted
the gallant warrior relapsed into gloomy silence, broken
now and again by a curse. But he was only one in a
thousand.
“ Thht collapse of Australia in the Ist innings of
the first test-match was a miserable business after the
18 previously totalled on that ground, and had it not
been for the magnificent stand by Trott and Gregory in
the second try, the reputation of this Eleven would have
been gone. As it is, a number of the papers now only
give them second or third place in the cricket reports
where they at first invariably headed the matches. ’
“ In view of the lack of form shown by Iredale
latterly, the selection committee had little choice but to
leave him out. It was a choice between Iredale and
Graham (for all the rest who were chosen had been doing
pretty well), and Iredale, who had been the more con-
spicuous failure, was left out.
v. has been unfortunate with his
hands all through the tour, received a severe iniurv in the
second over of England’s first innings during the test-
match. In stopping one from Grace he split his hand
badly m an awkward place, between the thumb and first
fn ! fer - Iredale fielded for him, but in hopes tbat he
could keep his wicket up while Kelly made a few runs he
went in last m Australia’s second innings, and though in
much pain, batted pluckily for over half-an-hour, practi-
cally with one hand.
“ The stand made by Trott and Gregory in the
nrst test-match must be considered one of the finest exhi-
bitions ever given in international cricket. No previous
partnership in these matches has put on as many as 221
runs, and, in addition, it was done without giving a
chance. The only semblance of a chance was given by
Trott to Hayward in the slips. The ball went low and
very quickly, and seemed to touch the ground and Hay-
ward s hands simultaneously. The fieldsman threw the
out U Ti/ Ut T f rott a PP ealed and the umpire gave him not-
out. This, of course, caused a lot of discussion. The
English press was divided ; Hayward and many of the
English players were certain it was a catch. I was watch-
lng very closely, and it was so very near one way or the
other that I should say that if cricket is to be plaved in a
sportsmanlike way a batsman must be got out more de-
thaU that b6fore he ff ° eS ‘ Trott Was 61 at the
“Gregory’s play this year in England certainly
stamps him as one of the finest bats in the world. Strange
that Abel, of Surrey, who heads the English batting
averages, should be another little fellow, about an inch
taller than Syd.
, ‘ ‘ Many complaints and discussions are heard as
to whether Jones does not throw when sending in his
fastest ones. His deliveries have now been passed by the
leading umpires, and it is unlikely that he will be no-
balled now. As a matter of fact, all swift bowlers are
accused of throwing more or less, and if Jones had not
proved successful, or had not hit a few of the batsmen,
we should have heard nothing about it.”
* * * *
The safest All-England Eleven procurable at
the present time can’t be expected to get rid of the Aus-
tralians for small scores, or to run up a very big score
themselves. Given fine weather, the Australians ought to
make a draw of the third test-match, if they don’t actually
win. This seems to be about the most promising A.E.E.
on recent performances Grace, Stoddart, Abel, Gunn,
Ranjitsinghi (or Jackson), Hayward, Lilley, J. T. Hearne,
Pougher, Richardson, Woodcock (or Lohmann). Yet it
includes only two batsmen who have scored a “ century ”
against the visitors, and not one bowler whom they haven’t
punished severely at one time or other. A second team
of almost equal strength might be picked from the less
“ representative ” players, but that fact doesn’t touch the
point that the best English cricket has little or no ad-
vantage over the best Australian ditto, except in being
older and more artful. Our Boys don’t, as a rule, carry
their top “ form ” into the old and artful stage.
* * * *
Australian cricketers had not won a test-match
in England since 1888.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —It was not the miners of Kal-
goorlie who, when the Australians were all out for 53 in
the first innings of the test-match, clubbed together and -
wired Musgrove to send the team home, etc. The miners,
to a man almost, of this field are proud of the team. The
senders of the wire were certain upstarts who, by some
chance, have got hold of more money than is good for
them, and duuno where they are.—Miner. Kalgoorlie,
July 19, ’96.
♦ * * *
Ed. Bulletin, —Your cricket-scribe tries to
excuse the English defeat in the test-match on the
ground that they had to play faster than the Australians,
and so lost their wickets cheaply. The team going in first
in England and scoring 400 runs is, judging bv past
experience, almost safe from defeat, and, as the English-
men had the first match to their credit, is it not unreason-
able to say that they threw their wickets away by hitting
so as to prevent a draw ; especially in the second innings,
which they started 181 to the bad ? Grace didn’t throw
his chance away by hitting—he was much slower than
usual ; and Briggs, who was “ sent in to stonewall,” was
bowled for a duck. Ranjitsinghi, the fastest scorer on the
English side, was the only really successful batsman. Now
for the other side, which doesn’t appear in The Bulletin :
“ There was an inexplicably tedious display for about an
hour. Quaife, who over-night had made 45, took nearly
three-quarters of an hour to bring his score up to 50; and
Bainbridge, who used to be a rapid sewer, was in an hour
for 7.” (The Cricket account of the Australians v. Mid-
lands match.) Only the other day, the champion county,
Surrey, took all day making 283, and the Australians
scored 367 for their day. Notts took all day getting 276,
and Gloucester the best of an afternoon getting 110 against
“ Our Boys.” The Australians have beaten all the coun-
ties and England, so they can well afford to do without
the praises of your scribe, as the strong tree may still toss
its plumes though a vagrant leaf has blown away.—Aus-
tralian.
• • * *
London Times had a leading article on the first
test-match. The article, of course, concludes with the
observation that this match has added several horse-
power to the silken bonds.
* * * *
Noticeable how the attendance on the second
day of a match between the Australians and English
local teams falls off if the former have a big lead, and in-
creases if the reverse.
* * * *
The Australians lost no credit by the defeat at
Bexhill, and the annoyance of it will no doubt serve to
tickle their energies. The maddest man in the crowd
must have been Harry Musgrove, who took a hand and
helped to lose the game.
* * * »
Much sounder play was noticeable in the Q.-
N.S.W. return than in their initial contest. And the
crowd showed a livelier appreciation —because the locals
piled up 15 as against 9 points. Sydney’s heavier weight
and splendid combination were the chief winning factors.
The tourist backs got few opportunities, and their efforts
were often nullified through smart following up. Hanna,
whom the Union wanted knocked out on account of undue -
aggressiveness in an inter-club match, shaped excellently.
The selectors showed commendable spirit in refusing to
be over-ridden.
* * * *
For a change, high-class football distinguished
Saturday’s Melb. senior matches. Fitzroy, expecting to
run over Essendon, played plucky, skilful and pretty foot-
ball for three-parts of the game ; then, losing heart and
head, went to pieces. Essendon won by sg. to 2g.
S. Melb. and Melb. had a hot contest, every
inch of the game being keenly fought. Given one sure
goal-kicker, South would down every other club in the
Premiership, its play at present being perhaps better
than ever before ; but 2g. 14b. to 4b. tells its own tale.
Collingwood won its Geelong match by Bg. to
5g., and St. Hilda, putting N. Melb. through 4g. to 2g.,
moved up to sixth position on the premiership list.
* * * *
What the sport has come to. All Newcastle-
matches postponed, bar one, because referees were unob-
tainable. Umpires now regularly patronise accident
insurance offices. There is more danger in being an)
official onlooker than a neok-or-nothing player.
* * * *
The noble game of football as played in Vic-
toria :—“ The woman ‘ barracker,’ indeed, has becomeone-
of the most objectionable of football surroundings. On
some grounds they actually spit in the faces of players as
they come to the dressing-rooms, or wreak their spite,
much more maliciously with long hat-pins. . . In the-
height of this some of the women screamed with-
fear, others screamed, ‘Kill him !’ At the close of the
struggle one of the gentle maidens who ‘ support ’ foot-
ball remarked regretfully that it was a pity they’! let
off ’ the umpire in the Geelong match, as they should have
killed him.”
* * * *
The attack of the women upon umpire Roberts
at N. Melb., the other Saturday, has upset an idea the .
present writer has long been hatching. The scribe was
about to suggest the advisability of training up female
umpires in the way they should go. The toughest male,
crowd would not be likely to fall foul of a female umpire,
to her serious discomfort, but the designer reckoned!
without the new women—the up-to-date She who is a
bit of a sport. She attacks the male umpire, tears off
most of his clothes, and lets the air out of him with hat-
pins, where his decisions displease her ; what would she
do with a referee of her own sex? Go to extremes, pro-
bably, and destroy her hat. . .
* * * *
Little publicity is being given to the Blackham
testimonial. Melb. C.O. has donated 50gs. Commencing
in 1870, the peerless wicket-keep has played in first-class-
matches since’74, and been in every ’Ome team from’7Bi
onwards. That Pennant match accident last season pre-
cluded selection for the present tour, and he will unlikely
don the pads again.
* * * *
The Ballarat people, who ridiculed Artist’s
chance of winning a big event under a heavy weight, evi-
dently knew more about the horse than the Millers could
bring themselves to believe. Artist cut a very poor
figure in the Caulfield G.N. Hurdle Race. The handi-
capper will have to take at least a stone off his back next
time he is entered in good company.
* * *
Resolute, a promising Derby horse in W. R.
Wilson’s stable, won the Malakoff Stakes flutter at Caul-
field without an effort.

Scene, Melb. brewery - office. Clerk, to
traveller: “ Who’s the present licensee of the
Great North-Eastern Hotel ? ” Traveller: “ Shan-
nassy, the brother-in-law of that woman who used
to keep what-d’e-call-’em’s place down there near
the Royal Saxon. I forget her name just now,
but she was married to one of the M'Brien
brothers, who turned insolvent. His sister died
the other day while he was out ploughin’, and one
of children died coming down in the train.
Oh, he’s all right, he had plenty money but lost
it all in speculatin’.” “Oh, d it; where’s the
directory ? ”
Melbourne is being canvassed for subscrip-
tions for another bell for St. Paul’s, which has a
great deal too much bell already and does its full
share in the brazen clangor which makes the good
city of Smellburst unfit to live in on a Sunday.
Victoria, despite the revival which has set in, is
still in a woefully bad way, and the brand of piety
which wants money for bells just now is of a pretty
crude description. Likewise, it shows a great in-
capacity to understand the fitness of things. The
church which preaches about the sweet and fault-
less music of Heaven and points its discourse by
pounding a horribly discordant tom-tom on earth
is an incongruity.

The troubles of a ‘ ‘ club doctor. ” A
Sydney-suburban Scawtch doctor, while receiving
patients in his consulting-room, was interrupted
by a savage call—“ You’ve got to come up at once
and see X. ; he’s dangerously ill.” “I’ll go when
I ve attended all my patients here,” “No, you
must come at once ; it’s a case of life and death.”
No, sir. I know the X’s. ; they’re always
going to die. There’s nothing much the matter
with X.” . . . Two hours later the doctor
(and please note that he was Scawtch) saw X. in
bed ; and, upon his entreaty, examined his right
Hb Mrs. X. being present. Turning to Mrs. X.,
the doctor (mark you, he was Scawtch) asked:
‘You have a family?” “Yes’r, six.” “Are
they at home?” “ Yes’r.” “Bring ’em all in
here. I’m going to perform an operation, and I
don t know how things may turn out. They had
better be present.” Six assorted kids were ranged
ip f) ne beside their groaning and trembling father.
Now, Madam, ” said the doctor (remember,
he was Scawtch), “ I must trouble you for a basin
of warm water, a clean handkerchief, and a darn-
ing-needle.” The kids began to sob as the lethal
instruments were brought into the chamber of
suffering. Swiftly the doctor (and he was Scawtch!)
pricked the boil (X gave a howl), expressed from
it about a pin’s-head of matter, and dabbed the
awful place gently with the wetted handkerchief.
(( Will he die, doctor dear?” asked Mrs. X.
“Sairtainly, when his time comes. But I’m
happy to inform you, madam, that the operation
has been entirely successful, and has been borne
by your husband with heroic fortitude. Put a
bread-poultice on the place, madam, and let him
eat and drink as much and whatever he likes.”
And he was a Scawtch doctor !

Australia’s Most-Prominent Explorers.
Grand Hotel, Spring-st., Melbourne,
March 7th, 1895.
To Herr Rassmussen,
Proprietor of the Alfaline Remedies.
Dear Sir, —It gives me great pleasure to inform
you that I have derived great benefit from your
celebrated Alfaline Remedies during my explor-
ing expeditions throughout Australia, and I con-
sider them invaluable to all travellers in the bush.
—Yours very truly, B. BUTTON,
(Formerly a Councillor, Brighton, England).
Witness : A. F. Beker, Manager, Grand Hotel,
Spring-street, Melbourne.
Mr. Button writes later:—“The Pills I had
from you were of great benefit to me and others.
In one case, when three hunters and trappers
were travelling about the ranges near my place on
the Rose River, one was taken very ill ; his mates
intended taking him in a waggon to Wangaratta
(about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor. I
gave him a few of your Pills. lam pleased to
inform you that having to pass their camp next
day, I found him up and pegging-out his skins
and looking well. This speaks volumes for your
valuable Remedies.”

A Wonderful Cure.
Herr Rassmussen. August, 1894.
Dear Sir, —In the year of 1871 I was eight days
in the water sheep-washing, and contracted rheu-
matism and stiffness in the joints, and have tried
all advertised medicines. When I came to Sydney
I had a great difficulty in walking, and went to
the Turkish baths, which did no good. Passing
your window I saw a testimonial from Mr. E.
W. O’Sullivan, M.P., with the same complaint as
I had, which led me to try your Remedies. On
Thursday before Good Friday I took the first of
your Pills; since then I have used 4 boxes Special
Powerful Alfaline Vitality Pills, 3 boxes Special
Powerful Alfaline Rheumatic Pills, with Liver
and Kidney Pills, and free use of your Alfaline
Eucalyptus Oil, and I am now, with the blessing
of God, as well in health as I was 23 years ago.
I can with great confidence recommend your
Remedies to anyone suffering from the same com-
plaint. May you long be spared under God to
be a blessing to suffering humanity.
I remain, dear sir, yours very gratefully,
JOHN KEAY.
Witness: Edward Hardy, proprietor Town
Hall Coffee Palace, 521 George-street.

The advent of the Dunlop tyre marked a new
? ra *cycle history, for it is no exaggeration to say that
rt did more to popularise cycling than the cycle‘itself.
The Dunlop tyre made the cycle a perfect pleasure-giving
machine, and multiplied the number of C3 r cle-riders by 5o!
It accelerated racing speed to such a degree that second-
raters riding Dunlops hummed away smiling from top-
notch ers who hadn’t them. During 1895 no fewer than
1251 world’s records, as well as countless national and
local records, were beaten by Dunlop tyres. While
conflict as to who are champions from lap to
24-hours contests, there is only one opinion as to which is
the best tyre—you can guess it in once—the Dunlop.*

SCHNAPPS.
It is not very improbable that the winners of
the coming double, Caulfield and Melb. Cups, started for
the Balaclava Stakes on Saturday. The field behii d New-
haven included Nada, Devon, Marusa, Alphine and
Malula, and considering that Nada was backed like a
dead-bird ” the Derby favorite threatens to prove a
smasher in the spring.
• * * *
Summerlea was reported by the knowing ones
but the Melbourne knowing ones have
a habit of not knowing much about “ Billy ” Jones’
cattle.
* * * *
Madge Gordon’s defeat in the first Sapling
Stakes, at the Y.A.T.C. meeting, was a considerable shock
to Mr. Seji. Miller, whose fondness for early two-year-old
racing will not survive many disappointments of that.
a 0”: A’l .Pernicious three-furlong bursts of last
August the Miller candidate was invincible.
* * * *
The defeat of Mr. C. M. Lloyd’s crusade against
early juvenile racing at the V.R.C. annual meeting by 84
to 81 wasn t unexpected, but the close call augurs ill for
the continuance of the system in Victoria. The mover
has all the argument on his side. Experience shows that
squibs seldom last long, and are useless at the stud
* * * *
Saturday s Bank Handicap sweep - money
scramble, at Warwick Farm, brought out a fine field of
26, and imported Dalmeny held his own against the Aus-
trahans. Well-backed all-round the week previous, the
little black wasn’t started owing to the moisture.
Valiant s 9.7 didn’t prevent him running a great race and
getting fourth. Favorite Newman now owes the nublic
many thousands. r
* * * *
The inclination to flatsidedness and absence of
staying-power attributed to Newhaven will be unre-
servedly withdrawn after his Balaclava Stakes win under
8.10. Both he and Resolute won their Caulfield races in
style on Saturday, though not near fit. As half-a-dozen
other Derby colts are said to have big chances, this year’s
riband should be more interesting than the Cup
* * * *
Another sacrifice to the god of amusement
Jockey W. M‘Leod and racer Teralba, both dead through
a fall in Warwick Farm Anniversary Stakes. Prince
Chester and rider, falling at the same time, escaped scathe-
less. The old story—“ the home turn.”
* * * *
The 42 seekers of fortune collared in a Melb
tote-shop averaged 3s. each. These wayside “plun°ing”
resorts are frequented mainly by the cream of loaferdom •
professors of language and tobacco, gleaners of informa-
tion, and pickers of the pockets of respectability.
In four seasons sensational Hova scored 12 wins
8 seconds, 5 thirds, and was 20 times unplaced The
retired champion never won a paltry maiden-plate, though
he started in three.
* * * *
Summerlea’s winter race at Caulfield was the
third consecutive National in a few weeks for which 13
started. Also, it was the third ring-victory over the
public who are always trying to take them down. Jockey
Jas. Hayes parted with Summerlea to W. T. Jones a
month or so back. A National and a Balaclava Stake
with two outsiders in one afternoon is very payable horse
amusement.
» * * *
Nada ought to have done better than third to
Newhaven on Saturday, but should be equal to a big cash-
prize later on. She is the most leniently treated of the
St. Alban’s string in future distance events.
* * * *
The first Cup eliminations provide little for
comment, sensational withdrawals being reserved for the
second and third acts. Bar The Officer, most of the
Caulfield malcontents were expected. In Melb. Cup 98
have been committed for trial, two-thirds of which won’t
see the post. Three-year-olds will likely play a big part
this year, the majority of the oldsters being ‘incapable of
winning even if they started the night before.
* * * *
Four-mile Caulfield G.N. Steeple this Saturday
will distress the top weights if there is anything to take
them along. The erratic M.L. pair, only arriving a few
days before, were wisely scratched. Songster may hum
home safely, but never was a race so open. The best ap-
parent plan is to back a light-weight with dubious cre-
dentials, such as Tayforth or The Louth—the latter for
preference, because he has got “no possible chance.”
* * * *
If The Parisienne is to win Sam Cook his first
C. Cup she will likely be saved instead of tilting for Sydney
Metrop. with 71b. more. Also, if The Capt, has any chance
in the Metrop. with 8.3, his Caulfield prospects w'ith 151 b
less must be esteemed. But he is a nice horse to lose
m rney on. Toreador will prove a warm article for Rand-
wick if commissioned, and it only needs the presence of
Wallace and Trentham for Sydney Cup to be fought over
again.
* * * *
Cabled that in a team-race Australian cyclists
Lewis, Megson and Payne, were easily beaten over one
and five miles by Englishmen.
Cyclist Parsons was to make his first appearance
on a European track at the Paris Velodrome, in a match
against time, a fortnight after the mail left. As no news
s performances have been cabled, it is safe to assume
that his try, if it came off, resulted in no record-smashing.
It is stated that Parsons has a good offer to tour the U.S.
next season.
* * * *
Dan O’Brien’s luck isn’t worth a bunch of
carrots. The fine striding Loyalty has now twisted a
fetlock, and will be sent ’Ome for sale. Fat-kneed Bob
Ray is weighted out of Melb. Oup. He is not a Paris.
» * * *
A Kalgoorlie dreamer has had a midnight vision
of Destiny winning Caulfield Cup. A Sydney sport
recently dreamt a grey horse won Melb. Cup. “I’ll lay
100 to 2s. 6d. that a grey won’t win,” said a bookie.
Wager accepted. There is only one grey in the race, and
he won’t start.
* * * *
Young Towns is losing many of his sculling
vagaries under Messenger’s tutorship at Double Bay. His
opponent, Neilsen, paddles his own canoe, disdaining a
coach, and is rapidly striking form. The “ Dutchy ’’ beat
Stanbury twice years ago, but wouldn’t race him now for
a box of matches. Towns, though, is ambitious, and in-
tends flying at the champion if he wins his match.
• • * >
“The last time I parleyed with Gaudaur,” says
Stanbury, “ he wanted me to row him at Aurilia, in the
backblocks of. Canada; a place noted for its ice, red
Indians and things. I wasn’t taking any ice in mine lust
then.”
* * * *
From a letter from one of the N.S.W. cvcling-
team now in England to a friend in Australia:—“London
is a wonderful place; an Australian doesn’t know he’s
alive until he has drunk the joy of the music-halls. There
is as much difference between Australian music-halls and
the London order as there is between wooden legs and
living ones. And the catering stuns creation ; London is
on a perfect quad when it comes to epicureanism, and
Aus. is on the bone-shaker of indigestion. I attended a
banquet given by the Simpson-chain people, and I am
sending: the menu to the N.S.W. Wheelmen’s League for
the surfeiting of their imaginations. We (Megson, Pavne,
a "d Lewis) are riding for the Rover Cycle Co. at a salary
j, j 0 a F ear ’ w Rh trainer, bonuses, and travelling ex’s
added. Carlisle, in his attempt to lower the John o’
Groats to Land’s End record, had 40 or 50 men engaged
pacing, feeding, &c.—cost £5OO or so. The cracks ride
*“• rna °hines ; gearing, 76 to 96. Pacing here is perfect;
the Dunlop Tire Co. has ten teams receiving £2 per week
per man doing nothing but pacing. We are regarded as a
good draw, and. will make money touring the provinces.
Catford track is a revelation : you bowl along its glassy
banked surface with scarce an effort.”

An old lady, residing in a Melb. suburb,
got a “ fair startler ” the other day. She was back
with her rent, and also with her time-payment on
furniture. A fortnight ago she visited Sydney ;
and, on returning, was staggered to find a vacant
allotment where her home had been. The furni-
ture-man had seized and stored the furniture to
save it from being seized by the landlord, fee said
—and the landlord, having a good offer, sold the
house, lock, stock and barrel for removal. It was
so sudden, the old lady said.
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books ; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, I ancy
Goods; 6, Toys; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 1.6, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long.*
The Southern Sentinel, a quarterly
journal of serious tendencies and much unconscious
humor, calls evangelist M‘Neillto book for daring
to threaten a Divine judgment on Brassey’s yacht.
M‘Neill stated that Providence would pause the
Sunbeam to smite a rock, by way of punishing the
Vic. Governor for throwing it open to the public
on Sundays, but the Southern Sentinel denies
the evangelist’s power to work Providence in this
matter. The S.S., having ransacked the Holy
Bible for evidence of the Lord’s sentiments, con-
cerning the Christian Sunday, feels convinced
that He doesn’t wish it to be kept holy. He never
ordered it to be a Day of Rest, nor called it by a
sacred name in His Word. In short, Sunday is
not the Sabbath, and —says the occasional publica-
tion—“fearful is the responsibility of the man
who, with an open Bible before him, stands before
the people and teaches them that God is going to
destroy them unless they keep Sunday as the
Sabbath.” About 20 columns of solid reading
matter have to be waded through before this con-
clusion is arrived at. As regards what ought to
happen to breakers of the real Sabbath, the
Southern Sentinel is vague and uncertain.
Clearly it doesn’t know. Nobody knows, expept
the Deity, who omitted to lay down a law upon
the subject in His Word. Moses did know, pro-
bably, but he is dead. Moreover, there is a doubt
as to whether there ever was such a person, outside
of the Bible aforementioned. Nothing seems cer-
tain, not even the fearfulness of M‘Neill’s respon-
sibility, or theterewthof the Southern Sentinel.

mO MINING COMPANIES, BATTERY OWNERS
1 AND OTHERS.
Increase your yields by Saving the Fine Gold
and Pyrites by the use of
Wynne and Tregurtha’s
Patent Battery Plates.
This Invention, lately patented, revolutionises the ordi-
nary battery treatment of ores, for by its use copper-
plates, quicksilver, and chemicals are entirely dispensed
with. The Wynne and Tregurtha Patent Battery Plates
are made of iron, cast with peculiarly-shaped ribs. They
are fitted in a wooden frame, and take the place of the
ordinary copper-plates and blanket tables. The tailings
on leaving the stamper boxes pass over the plates which,
from their construction, catch the fine gold and pyrites,
allowing the sand and water to flow away. When the
plates are fully charged with the concentrates they are
then tilted backwards by means of a lever, throwing the
contents into a trough underneath. In practical use
much better results have been obtained by these plates
than by the ordinary copper ones, the yield of gold being
increased by fully 10 per cent. Excellent results have
been achieved in the treatment of refractory ores, where
the gold-is associated with some foreign mineral, which
has proved deleterious to the mercury and causing the
loss of the gold. The plates are strong, simple in con-
struction, and easily handled, and can be obtained from

Apologues.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
A BOY AND A PUP.
He was fondling it when I first saw him. Such a
miserable-looking object —no tail, no hair, and
very little of anything else. .
It had been kicked out to die. He had dis-
covered it, and was tenderly nursing and petting
it back to life. Pity and love welled froin his
eyes. His soul gushed through them, and deep,
dumb gratitude gazed back. .
A few weeks later I saw them again. Ihe hair
had grown, and the pup was sleek and fat--oaf the
hoy was heating the pup.
TWO ANTS.
One was red ; the other black. They should have
been fighters, but seemed disinclined _to begin
when I poked one towards the other with a piece
of stick. The black was the greater coward and
ran for his life every time. I was viciously deter-
mined to make them fight, and persevered until
the red got a grip. _ n . ,
The black was getting the worst of it. W nat
right had I to force the poor wretches to torture
one another? The red pulled a leg off. The cruel
little demon was not fit to live —so I killed him.
Adelaide.
W.

“At the Last Moment."
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Swannage,” said Townsville, “come
found and give me a hand with the packing. Are
you busy'?
“No,” said Swannage. “I have seen all the
patients on my list this morning. But haven’t
you packed everything yet ? Why, the boat sails
this afternoon ! ”
“Oh, yes. I only left a few portmanteaux.
The res’t of my dunnage is on board by now.”
Townsville was not overburdened with remu-
nerative practice, and, with his wife and children,
was on the point of starting for a new field—Aus-
tralia, the supposed “ El Dorado ” of unsuccessful
men. ,
Old chums, these two. Easy-going, middle-
aged Swannage, with his level head, and Devon-
shire dogged good-humor, was Townsville’s
chiefest friend and confidant—next to his wife.
Poor old Swannage ! A bachelor, too, and to all
appearances likely to remain so to the end of the
chapter.
It certainly was a tug for Townsville to finally
“cut the painter,” particularly as Bessie had
never become exactly enthusiastic about leaving
“ Home ” ; (“ Home ! ” and a precarious living of
“two-fifty” a year to support two children and
“ keep up appearances ” with). But Bessie was
always “ entirely guided by Bob.” And Bob had
decided to go, and so
• • • •
“There! Now a last ‘nip/ old man.
Sit on this hat-box. We’ll both have to drink
out* of the same glass; all the others went in
‘lot 24.’”
“ Here’s luck, Townie, and to our next meeting !
Ha ! Ha ! I wonder when, and where ? ”
“Luck, Swan . If you weren't such a lucky
bargee, and so infernally popular with your
patients, I’d say ‘may it be soon.’ But as it is,
why, there’s a very small chance, eh ? I wish to
the Lord you were coming with us. Bessie’ll miss
you, I know. She thinks no end of you, Swan.”
“ Yes ? ” indifferently from Swannage.
“ I should rather think so ! D’ you know, old
chap, I was awfully near suggesting that she and
the kids should stay behind until I made things
comfortable out there ”
Swannage looked up quickly, “ Eh ? ”
“ Oh, I gave up the idea. I thought you’d have
looked after them, but, after all, it would have
been imposing too much on your good nature, and
I gave it up. It will no doubt be best to go
together; it’s all arranged now, anyhow.”
“Yes, of course,” assented Swannage.
Townsville rose and looked at his watch; “Stay
here while I fetch a cab, will you, Swan ? Bess
will be down in a minute or so. You’ll come down
to the boat with us ? We can all go together.”
“Yes,” replied the other, absently, “go
together.”
* * * *
A minute later the hall-door banged, and
Swannage was ascending the carpetless stairs. At
the first'door on the left of the dusty landing he
stopped for a second, looked at nis watch, and
entered without knocking.
* » * *
Mrs.. Townsville was looking out of the
window at the dreary prospect of suburban brick,
blurred by the rain which poured monotonously.
She did not turn as-Swannage entered. “ He has
gone,” she said, pushing her unbound hair back
from her forehead. She was riot dressed for going
out. Her hat and wraps lay tossed on the semi-
dismantled bed.
“ Yes,” said Swannage, as he walked to the
mantelpiece, and, taking a glass and water-bottle
therefrom, placed them on a small round table,
which, with the bed, made up the whole remain-
ing furniture in the room. He moved the table
near to the head of the bed. There was silence
for a space, save for the patter of the rain on the
window, and, more remotely, on the roof.
Then she turned and said, “Have you got it,
Jack ? Will he be long.? ”
He answered categorically Yes. No. About
five minutes.”
“Oh, Jack, darling, will there be time? she
asked, coming to him and showing the first trace
of emotion.
“ Plenty,” he answered. Then, filling the tum-
bler with water, he drew from his pocket a small,
fluted blue bottle, loosened the stopper, and
placed it also on the table. Then he turned
sharply. “ Bess, are you afraid ? ”
The woman smiled up in his face and shook her
head. “I was only afraid that you had not
brought it and that there might not be time,” she
said. _ , , .
“ And have you rio regret ?” he asked, his arms
round her now, and his nervous hands buried m
her thick, dusky hair.
“ Not one, love ; kiss me ! ” and she raised her-
self on tip-toe and offered him her lips, which
were steady and moist and smiling. “ And now,
she continued, gently unwinding his arms, ‘me
first, for fear ”
He moved to the table and poured the contents
of the bottle into the glass of water. There was
a sudden scent of almonds in the room.
She took the glass from him, seated herself
on the edge of the bed, and offered him her dis-
engaged hand, which he clasped in both his as he
knelt beside her on the bare, dusty floor.
(t Together now, always,” she said, and smiled
at him again; then putting the tumbler to her
lips she half-emptied it at a draught. .
He took it from her on the instant and drained
it. It fell and shivered to pieces on the floor as
their hands met once more, and then
* * * *
That is how Townsville came to alter his
mind at the last moment, and why he never went
to Australia. The last time I saw him he bor-
rowed five pounds from me. He spent it in
cocaine.

Item from our reptile contemporary in
Wellington (M.L.)
"WANTED, by a young man, comfortable Board and
VV Residence, within 10 minutes’ walk of G.P.O. ;
heathen family preferred. Terms, etc., to Pagan,
Evening Post.
Anybody who has tried board and residence with
a pious family will understand the situation. In a
pious boarding-house the evening meal is mostly
bread and scrape, and the boarders aren’t speak-
ing to each other, and the landlady hasnt much
hair, and wears it in a small knob on the top of
her head. And in the heathen establishment the
boarders play cards in the evening, and keep beer
under the table, and tip their cigar ash into a
saucer.
Darby and Joan: a Kodak.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A stuffy day in the Divorce Court. Three or
four barristers and a solicitor or two at the table;
behind, the usual nondescript crowd of cheap-
sensation seekers. A bronzed, pleasant-looking
man of about 35 in the box. He is the petitioner.
“Yes ! we’d been married six years. Happy?
Yes, we were known as Darby and Joan to every-
one in our district. She came up to Sydney for
the race - week —I had to stay at home for the
shearing. She remained six weeks —met co-
respondent : young globe-trotter, out here with
his yacht. One day, shortly after she returned, I
inadvertently began to read a letter that lay open
upon the blotting-pad, and realised everything.
It was from the co-respondent—he had been send-
ing her money ; she was preparing to join him. I
told her what I had learnt; that night she left—
and I have not seen or had word from her since.
She was last heard of on board co-respondent’s
yacht at Cannes.”
Then Joan’s father —a white-haired old man—
comes into the box to give some necessary evi-
dence. He breaks down and cries like a child.
More formal evidence.
No appearance for respondent or co-respondent.
Decree nisi.
And Darby ? ...
Darby went back to the station, and in six
months had nearly succeeded in drinking himself
to death —then he remembered the two helpless
little children, pulled himself together, took a
trip to Europe, and the other day married “the
sweetest woman in the world,” who is the best of
mothers to the little ones.
Darby’s all right !
Joan ?
She left the yacht not long after the divorce-
worried co-respondent to marry her—but he wasn’t
that sort. She was next seen in Paris, where her
beauty, dresses, diamonds, and her smart little
victoria were a good deal admired. She drove in
the Bois every afternoon —dined at the Cafe de la
Paix and the Maison d’Or, and was a regular fre-
quenter of the opera.
Then she commenced to age a little, and took to
laying the color on rather freely. She began to
slip gradually and almost imperceptibly down-
wards—Cafe Americain, Moulin Rouge . . . was
seen one morning in Piccadilly, in the small hours,
very dishevelled and slightly intoxicated.
Morganatic alliances —they end in the Morgue.
Vic.
J. H. WAGNER.
Cooee!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The parson had called during the day for sub-
scription purposes and found the wife and two
little ones crying at the bedside of the dying man.
Good-hearted, earnest, old Lee administered his
usual dose of hackneyed consolation, with his
usual firm conviction of its futility, but the tears
rolling down his rugged old face made up for that.
In the presence of a death fraught with such con-
sequences to the living, it seemed blasphemy to
murmur “ God’s will.” What was to be the
future for these three when the man on the bed
had gone? Before this ghastly conundrum of
Fate he stood benumbed —the conventional solu-
tion by Faith seemed to have lost its basis when
confronted with the embodied enigma. He quailed
before this —the Inscrutable and Inevitable ; and
by sheer force of habit —prayed.
Jack lay on the bed— the wreck of a splendid
man, tossed for 20 years on the fierce waves of the
Bush, and now entering the haven of which Death
is sole pilot. His was a lingering disease, one that
the Bush breeds in its dark dampnesses, and trea-
sures up for its own particular motives.. .Sleeping
anywhere and everywhere, toiling of necessity
through wet and dry, tramping alternately the
mud-and-dust-smothered treadmill of the Unt-
Back prison, he had grown weaker and weaker,
and then : the cowardly linker struck once with
all his force, and Jack’s strife with the Bush was
finished. “Man is not made of cast-iron . Ut
this truth, the pale, sunken face, the dreamy,
wide-open eyes, the muscular arm hanging limply
over the edge of the bunk, thin and lined with
ghastly blue veins, all bore grim witness.
“What’ll Kate do, Mr. Lee ? ” he asked slowly,
“ she can’t ever clear off the mortgage on the
ground-and Nell and Will, too! Oh God
Two tears told the intensity of his suffering, told
that he saw no beacon of Hope in the Future
for those he loved. The parson whispered to him,
and a look of relief followed. As night came on
he grew delirious, and uttered hoarse-whispered
ravings, shreds of memory from the 1 ast. Still
the old man sat beside him, whilst Kate m tearless
grief moved about the house, and Nell and her
little brother slept as peacefully in the corner of
the room as if their paths in life were free from
every obstacle, strewn by merry breezes with fresh
leaves, like the bridle-track down the hillside from
the house. , , , . ,
It was near morning when the dying man awoke
from a fitful doze, with the delirium gone—so the
watchers thought by the look on his face. He
raised a finger in warning and whispered :
“ Hush ! Did you hear that cooee ?
“ No.” Then it struck the old clergyman that
it must be the fancy of a dying man.
“There’s someone lost, parson! Gant you
li6£tr ’em ? 55
The old man went to the door: “ Yes.”
‘ ‘ Cooee back, man ! he’ll find the way then.
The parson gave a low cooee, choked short with
a cough. , . , . .
“Louder!” said the dying man, half-rising.
“ Louder, parson, for God’s sake. Ain’t ye ever
been lost in the bush ? It’s just hell. That s
what did forme —out on them hills all one night
—cooeeing—till—l was fair —done up. Louder,
man ! ” He fell back on the pillow.
Lee obeyed.
“ Is he coming nearer 1 ”
“ Yes, old man.”
“ Tell Kate to get him —some —tea.
But when he came no one wanted him to stay,
and Jack went out with him.
Via.

A Continental figure-juggler proves that
the most unlucky day for human accidents is not
Friday, but Monday, with 16.74 per cent. ; and
the least unlucky is Sunday, with only 2.69 per
cent. The descending order of fatality is—Mon
day, Friday and Saturday equal, Wednesday,
Tuesday, Thursday, Sunday.

A Coolgardie Wedding.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Let’s have another drink,” said the best man,
“She’s not in sight yet.”
The party, tired of waiting for the bride in the
heat, backed him up ; but the groom, nervously
brushing off the red dust that tinted his light
pants, refused.
“ He knows he might jib if he had another
drink ! ” was the general comment.
The church—a galvanised-iron structure —stood
on tire corner. The unlined interior showed the
sapling posts and beams, and the hessian backing
to an altar of kerosene-cases disguised with calico.
The company was more or less sober ; but the sun
was very warm. One man, through force of
habit, pulled out two pennies to start a two-up
school ; but he had just come in from Niagara,
and, as the best man said, “hadn’t the manners
of a blaekfellow.” In front was the dusty street,
with occasional whirlwinds waltzing up and down.
Over the wide area of heat-reflecting roofs the
tower of the brewery beckoned tantalisingly.
Man after man of the wedding-party made sudden
dives through variously-smelling backyards to the
bars .of Bayley-street.
Suddenly a cart turned the corner, and everyone
drew back, for in it was a coffin. The driver
pulled up at the church-door, and, utterly ignoring
the significance of the crowd, said to the bride-
groom :
“ Say, mate, give us a lift with this poor .”
The best-man explained that there wasa wedding-
party—but to no purpose. The driver of the dead-
cart said he had to “get his load prayed over,”
and hi's boss would kick up a row if he was too
long. Then the wife of the dead man and her
friends oame up in tears and put in their claim to
the church and the priest’s services. A heated
argument followed for precedence, while the driver
let one end of the coffin bump down on the path.
Between the ill-fitting lid and the sidehalf-an-inch
of daylight percolated, outlining the pinched edges
of a ghastly profile which had been jolted forward
against the lid.
“For God’s sake, man, shift back ! There’s the
bride ! ” said the best-man, pointing up the-street
where another cart appeared with a white blur of
dresses in the centre. The driver of the dead-cart
and his load were hustled into the lane, while the
bridegroom anxidusly asked whether it was fever,
and directed some one to “go for carbolic.”
Then the bride and her maids drove up. The
veils and dresses were patched with the inevitable
red dust; but thq. bouquets the ladies carried ex-
cited instantaneous envy.
“ Where did she get them lilies ?—are they lilies,
Maria ? ” whispered a feminine spectator only
lately married, who had to -be content with paper
flowers at her wedding. No, these were not paper,,
they glistened with dew, which made convincing
splashes on the dusty gloves, but no one could
recognise what flowers they were.
The party filed into the unventilated church,
which could never quite get free from the strong
breaths of the faithful, and was pervaded by an
unholy odour of tobacco and beer. The ceremony,
proceeded, the responses occasioning interruptions
by the driver of the dead r cart, who kept peeping
in and growling audibly '* W’en is this ’ere
circus going to finish ? ”
At last the knot was tied. The bride looked as
bashful as she could and took her husband’s arm
to march out, when one of the curious ladies
squeaked :
“ Lord ! I know the smell ! —they’re sliced
celery ! ”
The bride, furious, paralysed her with a look,
instantaneously resumed her bashfulness, and slid
gracefully through the sacred portal.
“ Our deal! ” said the driver of the dead-cart.
Goolgardie.
J. H. G.
Caught on the Beach.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In the spring of ’Bl, I was in Greymouth, M.L.
For a young man just freed from the heat of
North Queensland there was plenty to see and do.
The town is inconsiderable, but there is a noble
river to bathe and boat in. The bush-clad hills
and narrow limestone caves are delightful, while
to walk out along the mile-long breakwater and
watch the giant breakers rush surging in is an
experience not easily to be paralleled elsewhere.
One day old Tainui, the Maori “ odd man ” at
my hotel, started out for Point Elizabeth, a rocky
headland some six miles up the north coast, to try
a new lobster-pot he had made out of green supple-
jacks. We crossed to Cobden, an obsolete town-
ship on the north side of the Gi’ey River, and here
I foolishly gave old ' Tainui half-a-crown to get
some rum. He got the rum and carried it, walk-
ing just behind me,'and, after half-an-hour so,
:he calmly lay down by the roadside, handing over
tome with Maori gravity the empty rum bottle
and the lobster-pot and bait.
Mentally cursing the old fellow, I pushed on.
I had never been to Point Elizabeth, but it had
been indicated to me from the steamer’s deck,
so I knew I was right when, after a couple of
hours’ tramp, I arrived at a rooky island sur-
mounted at one end by a natural tower of rocks
crowned with.some tough bushes. The tide was
just low enough for me to reach the fishing pools
on the island, and in a few minutes I had baited
my trap, moored it securely in a nice deep pool,
and was quietly sitting down eating the lunch I
had brought.
1 had no lack of luck. Lobsters, or rather cray-
fish, were plentiful, and my first haul was three
beauties. I had forgotten to take the bag from
old T 'ainui; but I tipped them into a secure iso-
lated pool in the table of rock from-which 1 was
fishing. Soon I had a couple of dozen crayfish in
my extemporised aquarium, fighting and tumbling
over each other ; and, growing tired of such easy
sport, I started to explore the rest of the little
island.
Just then I discovered a curious thing. The
pool I had my crayfish in was at the foot of a
little wall of smooth rock some 12ft. high. Ex-
tending to within an inch of this wall, and in such
a position as to half cover the pool I was using,
was a huge flat piece of detached rock which, as I
clambered on to it, I found to be just on the
balance. It was, in fact, a “ rocking stone.”
* * * *
Here was a discovery. The mass was some 10
or 15ft. across, and by shifting my weight some
two or three feet each side of the centre I found I
could rock the stone to and fro iii a novel kind of
see-saw. The rocking-stone sloped sharply down
where it approached the little wall, and, going
over to examine that side of it, my foot slipped
from under me and I came bang against the wall.
The impact momentarily released the rocking
stone ; my right foot slipped in between the stone
and the wall; and, as I took the weight of my
body on my left, down came the stone again and I
was a prisoner !
I could not take my weight off the rocking
stone, for there was no crevice in the smooth wall
that rose above me to which I could cling. I
could not spring up to relieve the stone of my
weight, for my right foot was jammed, and my
left, being between it and the wall, was rendered
useless—even if the stone, instead of sloping
sharply down, had been flat enough to get a foot-
ing for a jump.
The pain, too, was intolerable. The space be-
tween the cliff and the stone was less than two
inches, and I had the prospect of being slowly
drowned as the tide rose.
Cursing my luck, I considered possible means
of escape. I had my knife, and if I could
cut away my boot I thought surely I could wrench
my foot free. I got out the knife, and, after a lot
of trouble, cut away my boot and sock. Then I
got as good a purchase as I could with my free foot,
and tugged and wrenched and tried to spring. No
use; the harder I pressed with my free foot the
greater the pressure on the rocking stone and the
tighter the jam.
So I waited and waited. My foot was, of
course, torn and bleeding, but so tightly com-
pressed as to be almost without pain. By-and-by
the tide began to come in, and I found myself
stupidly wondering how long it would be before it
reached my mouth ; and whether Tainui would
recover his senses and liberate me—or my remains.
I pictured to myself my skeleton, and wondered
whether my head would fall off first, or if I would
float off from where I was jammed.
Then I fell into a kind of dozing stupor, and
some hours must have passed, for presently I woke
to find the spray dashing over my face. Then I
discovered that feeling had come back to my foot.
Slowly I grew conscious of a cutting, pinching
sensation, and, looking through the crevices, I saw
—the crayfish were grappling and starting to eat
my toes !
The agony of that time ! I yelled idiotically
at. the brutes, forgetting they could not hear. I
tried to stamp with my free foot, and then, the
horror of the thing overcoming me, I cursed and
yelled like a maniac.
* * * *
Suddenly, during a lull in the booming of the
surf, the echo of my despairing coo-ee calne back
to me from the foothills. Tool that I was not to
have called out before, I thought; there might be
someone within range. I had lungs like leather,
and, quickened by hope and maddened by the
attacks of the lobsters, I sent out a succession of
coo-ees that fairly lacerated the atmosphere.
Just as I was on the point of desisting, a figure
appeared on the beach. I thought of Tainui, but
no ! the figure was a woman’s, and my heart sank
as I reflected that she would be powerless to help.
By this time the spray from the breakers was
flying over my head incessantly. No sooner, how-
ever, had the woman made out for certain that
there was someone on the rock than she set about
rescue. Quickly she discarded her short and
scanty costume and entered the surf. Cleverly
she breasted the rollers, taking full advantage of
the undertow and diving- through the worst of the
breakers, until she gained the rock, and came
stepping along its slippery surface.
As she neared me, I wondered. She was nearly
full-grown, her body and limbs displaying, the per-
fection of athletic womanly grace. She could not
be a Maori —or a half-caste, even—she was too
light. And she surely was not a European, for
slie betrayed no token of shame at standing thus
naked before a man.
Quickly she learned my plight, and like a Trojan
set herself to free me. She ran to the opposite
end of the stone, and, though the breakers were
now beating on it, tried to bear it down so that
my end would tilt up and release me. Her weight
was not enough, but as active mentally as she was
physically, she sang out, “ Hang out as well as
you can while I go for a sapling.”
Though every second seemed like a minute to
me, the novelty of this turn in the adventure took
my mind off the pain sufficiently to let me admire
the girl’s strength and agility. Lord, how sl e
swam ! Sometimes I would see her on the curl of
a breaker, side-arming ahead like a steam-engine.
Then, as the wave broke, I would catch sight of
her head as she shook her long hair back and
eramely breasted the undertow. Soon she gained
the shore and, wet and bare as she was, ran along
the beach out of my sight. When she reappeared
she had in her hand a tomahawk, and made across
the shingle bank into the scrub that bounds the
beach. She soon returned with a long, stout
sapling.
, How she lugged this heavy green stick through
the surf over to the island where I was ; how she
inserted the end in the crevice alongside my foot
and prized away that dreadful rock, must be
imagined, for I cannot describe it. What I know
is that she got me ashore just after dark, and, my
foot being fearfully injured (the crayfish took part
of two of my toes—old Dr. Morrice took the rest)
she extemporised a pair of crutches and took me
home to her father’s hut.
* * * *
Here, in exchange for iny own, I learned' his
history, which included, of course, that of his
half-wild girl: His name was Tregarthen—a Cor-
nishman ; had been a miner ; was a beachcomber
on the 17-mile beach. She had grown up what
Mrs. Grundy would call a regular heathen, till in
her 17th year, though she could neither read,
write, sew, or dress herself decently, and knew no
more of religion or civilisation than a troglodyte,
she could shoot, fall a tree, “ wash the black
sand, dig in the garden, and swim like an otter.
I stayed at the house until the packer brought
out a horse to carry me into town and to the hos:
pital. And now . . . now my plain-spoken
friends tell me that it was all a scheme—that I
went fishing for lobsters and caught something
that holds a good deal tighter even than a lobster
—a wife. However, as the same friends rave
about the beauty of my wife’s face and figure,' 1
am satisfied, because I, who first saw her shining
like a water-goddess on the rocks at Point Eliza-
beth, know that it is true.
BROGDEN.
